The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that can be achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Cooperative Societies
benefit some millions of persons—their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor sees not only a fair com-
mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply tins communistic principle to
and the CO-OPERATIVE IRRIGATION and MERCANTILE SOCIETY
of .AUSTRALIA, Ltd., have already secured three irrigation colonies in
Victoria, which are partially settled and planted, and are negotiating for the
purchase of a tract of magnificent irrigation country in the West. Nego-
tiations are also pending for a Government concession of Murray land, and
there are now
half-populated, awaiting the small Capitalist, the employed ARTISAN, or
the man whose sole CAPITAL is his LABOUR.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 29, 1891.
The Nomadic Voter, and Other Matters.
Day by day the question of the representa-
tion of the people gets into deeper water in
the New South Wales Assembly. The long
hours of incessant argument over Parkes’
famous Bill have led to nothing but soul-
destroying confusion. The purposes in-
volved in the Bill are sufficiently simple, but
the incurable tendency to spread a few plain
facts into 150 clauses, and to involve them
in a tangled forest of words and deceased
grammar has obscured it past all human
comprehension. What results the Repre-
sentation of the People Bill will ultimately
produce—whether it will develope into a
Land Act or a dog-tax ordinance—can only
be conjectured, but its presumable objects
are as follows : First, to provide that every
adult sane white citizen who is out of
gaol shall be registered as the proprietor of
one vote and one only ; secondly, to provide
that he shall vote only once ; and thirdly, to
ensure that the Legislature shall be elected
by the majority of the votes polled. It is
necessary to define these three purposes, for
the Bill itself explains them so badly
that they are only matters of specu-
lation. Possibly, if that unhappy measure
were illustrated with diagrams, showing the
voter going to the poll, and returning from
it after he has voted, and the returning-
officer counting the votes, and, finally, the
successful candidate making his entry into
Parliament, some light might be thrown on
the darkness, but the Parkes Government,
without illustrations, is incomprehensible.
* # #
The first essential in carrying out the
democratic principle is a definite system
of registration, and this part of the pro-
gramme admits of four distinct alternatives.
The first is a continuance of the present hap-
hazard process, whereby the voter grows on
to the roll rather than arrives there by any
definite method, and the chances for or
against any particular individual being on
the list are about as vague as the chances
for or against the same person being struck
by lightning. Under this plan, the dead
remain on the register till their names fall
off by a gradual process of decay, and no
definite results are ever arrived at. The
second alternative is a rigid system of official
registration, by which every voter shall be
annually inspected and enrolled by a public
official ; this is the device which is supposed
to exist at present in New South Wales, but
which certainly does not and never has
existed. The third alternative is voluntary
registration, whereby any qualified individual
who chooses to apply may have a vote, and
the rest of the nation will stand out; and
the fourth is a compulsory method, under
which every citizen must enrol himself once
a year, or incur a penalty for nonfulfilment
of his duty as a member of the Common-
wealth. fhe objections to all these plans
have been discussed until they are sufficiently
familiar. The first is incurably idiotic and
incomprehensible ; it is a shapeless policy by
which the voter is washed on to the roll bv
the sea of accidents, and remains there till
he is washed off again, and it serves no fixed
or definite purpose whatever. The second
is costly, and can never be made absolutely
efficient, and it leads to a helpless depen-
dence on the State, inasmuch as the citizen
who must have his vote brought round to
his door before he will use it will presently
want his candidate brought round also. The
third is also hopelessly inefficient ; and the
last device of the four is denounced as an
outrage on the liberty of the subject, and a
brutal encroachment upon the rights of man
in general.
* * #
Still the choice practically lies between
the two methods last-mentioned, and of the
two the latter is decidedly the less objection-
able. The system of voluntary registration
has one defect which is absolutely incurable.
The average citizen will always register
under sufficient pressure, and the well-
organised party, with abundance of funds,
which can afford to form committees, and
hire canvassers, agents, and inspectors all
over the province to rally up its supporters,
will invariably get ahead of the party which
has to depend on brains rather than on
money. The old property-vote will be
abolished, but property will remain the
ruling influence none the less, and in em-
bodying this device in his Electoral Act
Parkes has given all due consideration to
the fact that the party of property is his
party, and that any advantage which may be
gained will be Parkes’ own advantage, and
his alone. On the other hand, compulsory
registration would place all parties on an
equal footing. It would be, undoubtedly,
an outrage on the liberty of the subject if
the subject, in this particular sense, had any
liberty to be outraged ; but such liberty is a
tiling of the past, and therefore this objec-
tion is bogus and altogether void. The
liberty of the subject disappeared with the
beginning of civilisation. The law compels
the individual citizen to register himself and
his family, his horses, his agricultural imple-
ments, and all his other property in the
decennial census. It requires him to register
his marriage, the birth of his child, and the
death of any member of his household. It
demands that he shall register his dog,
and it inquires about his income for purposes
of taxation, and it infringes his liberty in the
case of any outbreak of contagious disease in
his family. It keeps him busy, in short,
registering all manner of different things
from the cradle to the grave, and amid so
much compulsory registration one more
registration can make no appreciable differ-
ence. And, by and bye, in all probability,
the law will interfere still further with the
liberty of the subject by making voting also
compulsory. It already tines a citizen when
he refuses to have his head broken by assist-
ing in the arrest of some brawny ruffian
several times his own size, and in the face of
this outrage the other is too insignificant to
be worth objecting to.
# 3F
The abolition of double-voting and per-
sonation is a matter of comparatively little
difficulty. By holding all elections on one
and the same day—this, let no one forget,
is an absolute democratic essential, for a
variety of reasons—double-voting can be
provided against to a very great extent in
country districts, for even the fraudulent
elector, who has, by perjury, obtained an
electoral right in two separate con-
stituencies, will be so efficiently blocked by
the difficulties of transit that deception of
this kind will bo practically extinct. In the
city, on the other hand, where frauds—
electoral and otherwise—have their most
congenial habitat, double-voting can only be
put down by a rigid system of organisation.
The division of the city into single elec-
torates, the subdivision of the electorate
into wards, and of the wards into precincts
—upon the American plan—of two or three
streets each, the multiplication of polling-
booths, and the appointment of delegates
living in each precinct and personally
familiar with its inhabitants to repre-
sent each precinct at its own poll-
ing - booth, will probably meet the
emergency better than any other device.
Then each elector can be identified with
almost certainty by the delegate from his
own precinct—a system which will reduce
the great electoral swindle to the lowest
possible minimum. Moreover, under a
system of compulsory registration and con-
stant supervision there Avill be no dead men
to personate, and by the abolition of the
Sydney graveyard-vote one great element of
fraud will be completely eliminated.
# #
The avowed object of an election is to
secure a Legislature which has been chosen
by an absolute majority of the electors.
Hitherto no such Legislature has ever
existed in New South Wales, and it can
only be secured by live drastic reforms : the
abolition of the property-suffrage, compul-
sory registration and compulsory voting, the
system o.f second ballots, and by the holding
of all elections on one and the same
day. The object of the last-named
measure is partly to make persona-
tion impracticable as already mentioned,
and partly to abolish that peculiar system of
lawful and legitimate political fraud at which
the Parkes Government has proved itself
such an adept. In a general election that
dark and scaly cabinet invariably takes the
vote in the reliable Foreign-trade con-
stituencies first, and places the hostile con-
stituencies at the extreme end of the list.
By this device, it opens the campaign with a
long list of successes and gains valuable
prestige ; it enables its unsuccessful candi-
dates to stand a second or even a third time,
and sets its successful nominees free to
stump the country with garbled statistics
and strange, baseless assertions ; and its
opponents meanwhile come in at the extreme
end of the programme, and, if defeated,
have no second opportunity. This is not
absolute swindling, neither is it bribery and
corruption, nor intimidation, nor any other
indictable offence, but it is an underhand
proceeding which secures the advantages of
all three combined. The object of com-
pulsory voting is to abolish the entire system
of party organisation, whereby the faction
which can spend large sums of money to
whoop up its supporters invariably counts a
largo vote at the polls, and the party which
is short of money does not. For although
open bribery may be practically extinct, solid
coin is still the ruling influence in the State.
The methods by which a party is organised
are lavish advertising, the hiring of expen-
sive orators, the subsidising of newspapers,
the distribution of impassioned printed
addresses, the giving of banquets, and similar
means ; and even a minority which has been
vigorously canvassed by these methods can
generally get ahead of a majority which has
not been aroused to superhuman activity by
any banquets or lavish printing or expensive
oratory, or other outliy of superfluous riches.
Compulsory voting, in fact, is the inevitable
outcome of compulsory registration, and
they arc both the natural concomitants of
the system of election by absolute majorities
and the abolition of plural voting. They
have all one well-defined object—to abolish
not only open fraud and intimidation, but
also the entire system of shuffling, evasion,
official “faking, ’ subterranean engineering,
legalised swindling, and passing on of the
responsibility, and to make the Legislature
represent the absolutely untrammelled
opinion of a definite majority of the nation.
# * *
The objects of a truly democratic Electoral
Act may be summed up in one sentence—to
make all men free and equal. And, by way
of doing this, it is essential to abolish party
organisation, as it is at present understood,
the same being merely an underhand device
whereby one section of the community steals
a march upon and smothers another ; to
abolish plural voting; to establish the system
of second ballots in order to do away with
the nefarious swindle of nominating fraudu-
lent candidates on the enemy’s ticket in
order to split the hostile vote ; and to extin-
guish the equally nefarious, fraud of pro-
tracted elections, which are almost invariably
engineered to suit the convenience of the
party in power. So far the course is
sufficiently simple, and but for the sufficient
fact that Pakkes’ democratic principles are
bogus, and his Liberalism a hollow sham,
the whole matter could have been provided
for in an Act of twenty or thirty clauses.
* * #
The one great difficulty which has so far
proved practically insuperable is that of
dealing with the nomadic voter. In former
times this question was one of small import-
ance. The sailor, the shearer, the pros-
pector, the hawker, the Murrumbidgee
whaler, and other unsatisfactory children
of the wilderness and the sea" were all
individuals without property, therefore the
fact that they had no residential qualification
and no vote was of the very smallest account.
But the Australian democracy is at last
beginning to weary of the reign of bricks
and earth. They realise that every adult
sane white citizen is entitled to vote because
he is a citizen, and not because of his
residential qualification, or any other qualifi-
cation of the same kind—in fact, that the
residential qualification is not the cause of
the vote, but merely a handy means of
identifying the voter. Moreover, the Go-
vernment is always ready to collect taxes
from the seaman, and the shearer, and even
the " whaler —provided it can get them ;
and if ever this country is reduced to dire
straits by foreign invasion the shearer and
the stockman and the “ whaler” and the
others will be swept in by the same levy cn
masse which takes in the affluent inhabitant
with a large stomach, and a “stake ” in the
land. And further, if the seaman'"and the
“ whaler ’ and the rest of the nomads do
little good to the country, they, at least, do
no positive harm, and for that very reason
they are better entitled to- a share in the
Government of the country than the pub-
lican who makes Ids own grog, and the man
with the 60 per slient bank, and the stock-
exchange bird of prey, and many more. But
still the nomadic voter is a problem that is
hard to solve.
* * #
He belongs to no particular constituency,
and is attached to no particular electoral
roll. He can’t be identified, as a rule,
in order to guard against double voting
or personation. It is almost impossible to
find out if he is dead and is being resurrected
for purely party purposes. It is almost
equally hard to ascertain if he was ever
alive, or was merely an, imaginary and bogus
voter from the first, and if he °is
found out, it is another serious problem
how to arrest him and bring him to justice,
seeing that he is a man with no address.
He can’t be traced by bis handwriting, for
he all writes Very much alike, or else makes
a smudgy cross on his ballot-paper. He can’t
be identified by Ids photograph, for he can
give false names, and any name will fit th >
photo, of an unknown man. The system of
compulsory registration will make it neces-
sary to furnish extraordinary facilities f o >.
obtaining electoral rights; and when tin
voter is allowed to register at eveiw
country post-office and police-station, then
the nomad—who can’t be identified— can
take out a fresh electoral right at every town
he visits. And being thus registered as (savt
John Smith of every separate township m
the province, he can next come down to
Sydney, where the constituencies are closely
massed together* and sell his vote by virtue
of fifteen elector’s rights obtained at fifteen
different places, as fifteen separate and indi-
vidual Smiths, and make much hard cash by
the process. Yet it is impossible to crush
out this crime by inflicting a heavy penalty
on any individual found with more than one
electoral right in his possession, for then the
plural voter will be careful not to carry his
multiplicity of voting-tickets about him, and
the person who is convicted of this offence
will be some unhappy individual who has been
hocussed by an enemy, and got his pockets
filled with tickets by some one who desires to
walk out with his best girl while he is in
gaol. In every way the nomad seems to be
an insoluble puzzle. Possibly he might be
arranged on a satisfactory basis if the Return-
ing officer tattooed him blue after he had
voted, as it would then lie impossible for
him to efface the mark in time to vote a
second time at the same election ; but he
would probably object to this simj le and
efficient device. In short, the one absolute
certainty about him is that, being a white
man and a brother, he is entitled to a vote,
but exactly how he is to get it no one knows
just now. But he is going to get it.
“ A Challenge to Premier Parkes.”
The Bulletin has some questions to ask on a
matter of grave Australian interest. In the
first place it wishes to know what are the prin-
ciples which regulate prosecutions for criminal
libel in New South Wales? What is the
distinguishing feature that lifts a defamatory
statement out of the category of civil wrongs,
entitling the injured person to recover damages
in an action, and converts it into an offence
against the community, punishable by the
strong hand of the criminal law ? When does
an assault on a person’s character and reputa-
tion, like an assault with intent to do him
bodily harm, cease to be the private business of
the individual and commence to become the
affair of the State ? Presumably there are
definite rules on the subject, and The Bulletin
would be glad if some person in authority
would state for the information of the public
what they are, In the meantime some infor-
mation may be deduced from the decided cases.
A very short time has elapsed since Reseigh
Martin was criminally prosecuted for a libel
on Mr. Eddy, one of the New South Wales
Commissioners for Railways. The libel was
published in some obscure provincial newspaper
—at this moment we forget the name of it—
and consisted of a repetition of certain rumours
affecting Mr. Eddy, which, though conclusively
disproved by that gentleman, were at the time
undoubtedly current in the city and throughout
the country. The libelled man might, if he
chose, have obtained redress by a civil action
against the author of the article and- the pub-
lisher of the newspaper, who could, had they
failed to pay awarded damages and costs, have
been sent to gaol for a year. He did not take that
course, but a public prosecution was instituted;
Martin was proceeded against by the Attorney -
General in the august name of Her Majesty,
and, being found guilty, was sentenced to two
years’ imprisonment, which he would have
served to the bitter end, had not the Executive
been reluctantly compelled to remit part of the
sentence, in obedience to the overmastering
pressure of public opinion. Now, whatever
may lie the ingredient that is necessary to make
a libel a crime, it must have been present in
the circumstances of that attack against Mr.
Eddy. If Reseigh Martin was a criminal
and a criminal he was adjudged to be then
every individual who acts in the same way,
under similar circumstances, is a criminal, and
should be treated as such.
* # •
In lace of that case, we demand to know why
no steps have been taken by r the authorities
responsible for public prosecutions to bring to
justice the author of the gross, malignant,
and unparalleledly audacious string of un-
doubtedly criminal libels upon the Premier
of New South Wales published in a certain
Sydney newspaper on Sunday week ? Nearly a
fortnight has passed, and idl nothing has been
done, it may be assumed the publication is to
be allowed to pass-unnoticed. We do not chdose
to repeat the statements made about Sir Henry
Parkes, or even to indicate their nature. By
this time they are quite sufficiently public pro-
perty. Whether they are true or not we do
not know ;we do not now care to know. All
we do want to know is why, since Martin was
declared to be a criminal, and was punished as
a criminal, other criminals of the same type are
not treated in the same way ? Are we to sup-
pose that it was to satisfy some private grudge
that Martin was picked out and made a scape-
goat ? What is the secret that gains other
wrongdoers this invidious immunity ? Hoav are
wc to account for the fact that the Attorney-
General who rigorously prosecuted Reseigh
Martin allows John Norton to go scot free ?
* * *
Shall we be asked to believe the old, old
story that the members of the Parkes Govern-
ment do not read the newspapers ? Is the
publication in question too obscure to have
come' under the notice of the authorities ? Or,
will it be said that its obscurity palliates the
offence, and makes it too contemptible for the
law to take notice of ? That explanation is no
longer admissible. The paper boast a that it has
a circulation of 28,000 a week. The paper that
libelled Mr. Eddy was a petty country
sheet, usually only heard of in its own


little districts If the range of dissemination of
the slander is a material clement in the case,
then tli is offence is a thousand times more
heinous than the one for which Martin suffered.
Yet, no notice is to be taken of it. Who, we
ask, is responsible for this glaring display
of favouritism ? It would be intolerable,
it is hardly conceivable, that the caprice
or partiality of the law-officers of the
(Jrown should determine whether a given act is
to bo treated as a mere harmless peccadillo or as
a grave crime. To justify their action in
Martin’s case and their inaction in this, they
must clearly show an essential distinction
between the two. We challenge them to point
to any distinction that is not overwhelmingly in
Martin’s favour. Will they say that the alle-
gations made about Sir Henry Parkes are less
serious than those which brought the criminal
law to Mr. Eddy’s aid ? Reseigh Martin was
sent to a country gaol in irons. Had he said of
Mr. Eddy what this paper says of Sir Henry
Parkes, then, if the punishment had been pro-
portioned to the nature of the libel, no dungeon
in the country could be found deep enough —the
sacred relics of the palmiest days of the old
convict system could not supply irons weighty
enough —to make the scales hang even in the
hands of outraged j ustice. The article is one long
series of the most slanderous imputations, con-
veyed not by innuendo, not in ambiguous terms
capable of being explained away, but with
deliberate, explicit, categorical, circumstantial
audacity, coupled with an insolent challenge to
the Premier to take either civil or criminal
proceedings !
* * *
By way of justifying the prosecution of the
libeller of the New South Wales Commissioner
for Railways, it was urged that it was essential
to the due discharge of high public functions
that the officer entrusted with them should be
shielded by law from unwarranted attack. The
position occupied by Mr. Eddy was so exalted
and honourable that his character was a matter
of national concern. To impeach the honour
and integrity of so distinguished a public ser-
vant was to make an assault upon the body
politic and cast a slur upon the commonwealth.
That was why Reseigh Martin was pursued as
a criminal offender—not to redress Mr. Eddy’s
personal grievances, but to punish a wrong that
had been done to the State. Well, is Sir
Henry Parkes’ character a matter in which
the people of New South Wales have no
interest? Is he too insignificant and obscure
to come within the official cognisance of
the Attorney-General? Why, is he not at this
moment the most conspicuous figure in the
land; the Grand Old Man of the continent,
the doyen of our representative institutions, the
father of the coming federation, the George
Washington of his counti’y, the man whom
Her Majesty has been graciously pleased to
raise to high honour, a magnate of a knightly
order, a prospective Privy Councillor, the
first of an impending creation of Australian
peers! He.is more than all this: he is the
Premier of New South Wales, the chosen leader
of a great section of the Australian nation.
Is the head drayman of the province, the
manager of the public parcel delivery, to be
put on a higher pedestal than the man who
stands at the head of the country, who for all
representative purposes, is the country, who is
accepted all over the world as the country?
.Shall the State upraise itself in all the grandeur
of outraged majesty to crush the miscreant who
dares to utter a depreciatory word of Mr. Eddy,
and sit with a vacant smile of supercilious un-
concern while Sir Henry Parkes is spat upon
and trampled in the dust ? Again we ask, why
is this particular publication treated with this
.marked deference and consideration ? Why
:should one man be served as a felon and
.sent to herd . with felons for repeating
iin a back-block fly sheet with a limited
local circulation, rumours which, though
■absolutely without foundation, were in many
men’s mouths, affecting the character of a
subordinate official, while a metropolitan paper
is allowed, with impunity, to fling the vilest
charges with brazen defiance in the teeth of the
personage who fills the highest office that the
counti'y can bestow and to scatter its foul
slanders all over the world ?
# # *
It may, perhaps, be said that Sir Henry
Parkes prefers to take the magnanimous course
■of ignoring this attack. As to that, we may
;say, in the first place, that we do not put over-
much trust in the gentleman’s magnanimity.
■Some time ago another Sunday paper, conducted
(by Edward Eleis, so far forgot itself as to
make damaging reflections on the present
Premier. No steps were taken against the
paper directly, but the city police promptly
began a ruthless raid on the little boys selling
it in the streets; and musty, disused, ante-
diluvian statutes were resurrected for the
special behoof of the desperate young ruffians.
We accept implicitly the statement that the
Premier and the then Government had no part
in those proceedings, and that the coincidence
of the sudden back-handed persecution of the
journal in question at the moment when it com-
menced to say nasty things about the head of
the Government was due solely to .Sab-
batarian zeal of an officious constable. Never-
theless, we do not adopt the magnanimity
theory with any heartiness. Further, we say
that it has nothing to do with the matter. This
is a criminal offence we are talking about; and
when a man becomes amenable to the criminal
law, the course of justice should not be dis-
turbed by the magnanimity of any individual.
•So far as Sir Henry Parkes is personally con-
cerned—his right to recover damages for the
injury done to his reputation —we have nothing
to say That is distinctly bis own business.
He is entitled to put what value he chooses on
bis own fame and character. He may consider
that they stand too high to lie damaged by any
traducer ; it may be that he shrinks, as a man
of his age pardonably might, from the expense
and annoyance of an action at law ; it may be
that in a spirit of pure, generous Christian for-
bearance he is willing to forego his remedy.
Let that be so. That does not affect the duty
of the Attorney-General to set in motion the
machinery provided by the Constitution for the
repression and punishment of gross, flagrant,
find undisguised violations of the haw.
Tiie. Bulletin does not desire to sail under fa 1 s 3
colours in this matter. It would be a thrice
transparent affectation for us to pretend that we
were actuated by any strong feeling of regard
for Sir Henry Parties. Our sentiments
towards Sir Henry Parties are pretty well-
known ; we have been at no pains to conceal
them. We have no love for him, personally or
politically. The influence that he has exercised,
and is likely to exercise, on the destinies of
this country, we look upon as being almost
entirely for evil. If a word from us would dis-
miss him from power, and banish him utterly
and for ever from public life, we would speak
that word, not only without compunction, but
with a sense that we were discharging a sacred
duty and conferring a lasting obligation on
N.S. Wales and the whole of Australia. We
have not the hypocrisy now to pose as his
champion. If he feels aggrieved by what has
been said about him, he, with the machinery of
government in his hands, is perfectly well able
to take steps to right himself without the
assistance of The Bulletin. What leads us to
interfere in the matter is not that a slander has
been uttered against Sir Henry Parkes, but
that a slander has been uttered against the
highest dignitary of the State, anti through
him against the State itself. Australia is being
disgraced by the perpetration of a criminal
offence right under the nose of Attorney -
General Simpson.
* * *
What possible excuse can the Attorney-
General have for neglecting what, under the
circumstances, is his obvious duty ? What are
his colleagues about to allow him to neglect it ?
It cannot be that they consider the charges
made against their chief too unimportant to
warrant notice. Would Mr. G. B. Simpson
allow the same statements, if made concerning
himself, to go unanswered and unpunished ?
Would Mr. Bruce Smith? Would Mr.
Brunker? Would Mr. Suttor ? Would any
other member of the Ministry? If not, why
should they permit them to go unchallenged
when made about Sir Henry Parkes? It
would be insulting to ask if they suppose that
the statements could possibly be true. It
would be an insult to the Premier, an insult
to the province of which he is Premier,
and an insult to the members of the
ministry themselves. We repeat that we know
nothing about the truth of the statements, and
desire to know nothing. It is at present none
of our business. It is the Attorney-General’s
business, and he has given and continues daily
to give the clearest and most unmistakable
denial to every word of the whole calumnious
tirade, a denial expressed in the most-emphatic
manner by his presence in the ministry of
which Sir Henry Parkes is the head. No one
would dare to suggest that Mr. George Simp-
son, and the other gentlemen of high character
we have named, would remain for a single day
associated with a man of whom such things
could be even reasonably conjectured. it
must be taken, then, that the charges are as
false as they are cruel and malicious, and in
that case the individual who has made them
is a dastardly scoundrel on whom mercy would
be thrown away. The precedent we have cited
shows conclusively that he is also in the eyes of
the-law a criminal, and this on the authority
of the Attorney-General himself, backed Tip by a
judgment of the N.S. W. Supreme Court. It is a
crime to which is allotted a heavy penalty. W e
wish, therefore, to know —and the country has
aright to know—what mighty influence there
is in the background, paralysing the arm of the
law, and sheltering this particular criminal
from the punishment which he so insolently
and defiantly invites ?
Colston, Insanity and Deterrents.
Dear Bulletin, —Colston has been hanged 1
And considering his execution from a logical
standpoint, he must be regarded as, in the first
place, a victim to an outworn, rough-and-ready
and uncivilised and unscientific law; and, in the
second, to an ill-digested and blundering defence.
His counsel endeavoured to prove that the
unfortunate man was suffering from a mental
disease physically discernible, rendering him
in point of fact idiotic ; whilst the testimony of
experts, and Colston’s own intelligent conduct,
bear witness to his possession of sufficient
reason to enable him to judge between what is
conventionally regarded as right and wrong.
All his actions show that, so far from being in
the least degree insane, he had the fullest com-
mand of his faculties, and any effective defence
of his life should therefore have been based on
an absolutely different ground—that he was the
victim not of hopelessly diseased perception
but of irresistible impulse. Had the facts of
the case, with due emphasis upon all the
extenuating circumstances, been placed un-
reservedly before the jury, their finding of
Colston’s guilt might, supposing them to be
possessed of reasonable intelligence, have re-
ceived as a rider a strong recommendation to
mercy with a fair expectation of such
recommendation being seconded by the presiding
Judge, whose remarks on the hearing of the
appeal show him to be seized of the illogicalness
of the present legal definition of insanity. The
Executive would probably have favourably con-
sidered such a recommendation, based, as it
might have been, on sufficiently extenuating
circumstances, and spared Colston the final
office of the law.
# # #
As might have been the defence
broke down utterly. The whole question,
indeed, turns upon the legal and social
definition of crime, and—on parallel lines
the legal and social definition of insanity.
No doubt, when' Colston committed the crime,
exasperated though he may have been to the
extremest, he was just as insane as any other
man who has committed, or may commit, a
crime of like nature. It is not a question
whether the individual criminal is insane at the
time of committing a crime, so much as whether
every man who commits such a crime is not in-
sane. Nay, more, whether every man who has
committed no crime whatever, may not have
already deeply implanted within him the germ
which may, under certain conditions, not of his
own making, blossom into crime—and insanity.
For, looked at in a broad way, all crime is in-
, anity, and conversely till insanity is crime.
Samuel Butler is no paradoxist when, in his
“ Erevvhon,” Lo makes his ideal community
punish with dreadest Draconism all forms of
physical disability, and likewise with tenderest
solicitude all forms of dangerous scoundrelism
and direst villainy. The criminal is a sick
man. Havelock Ellis, in “The Criminal,”
treats the sinner purely as a product, an atavistic
monster, a throwback to the virtues of a past
age. Ellis’s social enormities stalk through
his pages as approximations in a latter day to
the heroes of the Homeric cycles. He doesn’t
belong to us—the criminal—he is an abnormal
type, out of the modern running, the blood
brother of the genius and the lunatic, and like
geniuses and lunatics deserving of a special
treatment and a special code of morality. Men
commit crime because they mmt, and until
society recognises this fact all criminal'‘juris-
diction must perforce be tentative and purely
expedient. Every year the statistician submits
undeviating tables forecasting the total of
drunkards, suicides, murderers, and people who
trill post unaddressed letters. These things
follow natural laws. There is little appreciable
divergence —yet we hang Colston to prevent
other Colstons from doing likewise !
». * *
It is a mere matter of moral cowardice. We
are afraid to accept the conclusions of that very
Science we are so prone to laud. M e
build inebriate asylums for confirmed
drunkards because we recognise that the
drunkard is a “patient.” We have not yet
advanced far enough to desist from punishing
attempted suicides because we are as yet too
slow to accept the conclusions of our own logic
to recognise that the suicide—who evades all
moral and social obligations in a spirit of appa-
rent egotism because he must —is also a
“ patient. ” By and bye we will do this. Then
the murderer’s day will come, when he, too,
will meet with a hospital and a doctor—instead
of a gallows and a hangman. It is merely a
question of logical acceptance of conclusions,
logically arrived at from premises logically laid
down. To-day, due doubtless to a spurious
revival of gallantry opposed to a Woman’s
Rights agitation, we dare not hang a woman.
We present a gloved hand instead of a hempen
necklace to the abortionist, and Mrs. Taylor
(who was to have been Colston’s gallows-mate,
and who, it is safe to say, has, for mere money,
committed a hundred deliberate crimes as
compared with Colston’s one dark deed
of uncontrollable and unsordid impulse)
laughs at the gallows, because a petticoat
hanging from a clothes-line shocks, not our
humanity, but our aesthetic sense. This may
lie hailed as progress ! Bah ! It is only a
resurrected form of an anthropoid belief in
woman as Property, and it is looked upon as
sinful to injure property :—a woman, therefore,
doesn’t pendulate ! A man is nobody’s property
but his own (and his landlord’s) ( Alston hangs;
It is time we looked the situation squarely in
the face. There are two types of criminals.
There are two types of crimes. And punish-
ment prevents neither ! There is the crime
which Springs from the possession of too much
virtue —social virtue, which rests on the instinct
of solidarity—which impels a man to violence
because all his powers and energies revolt
against iniquity exercised in opposition to all
that is most sacred to his emotions as a member
of a race with emotions worth preserving. To
prevent this man from so-called crimes of
violence you must first corrupt him —lover
his standard of virtue —punishment is here
powerless. There is next the instinctive
criminal, the horn criminal, the man who is no
good and who doesn’t know it, for his badness
is the goodness of a past age. The only attempt
of a scientific nature ever put in operation to
eradicate the inherent vice in him is in America,
and that at the Elmira prison. There is no
good in the world pooh-poohing the treatment.
Facts are too eloquent! The returns for further
treatment of prisoners, discharged from the
Elmira prison college, show only 1") per cent,
out of over 2000 discharges. The system is
eminently practical. It is based on the most
scientific principles. It recognises the atavistic
criminal, and it rushes his intellectual develop-
ment over gaps which would be bridged only
by centuries of slow social educational torture,
if he were returned to Society as ignorant
an animal as when he entered the peni-
tentiary college. Here is a fact, and any
community which evades its consideration is
cowardly and illogical. The Elmira experiment
has proved that education can- cure crime. If
one form of crime, why not every form —pro-
vided the crime is instinctive and not emo-
tional? Hospitals are open to the drunkard;
inebriety is no longer regarded as a sin. Theft
is even sagely pondered over as kleptomania
when the area-thief wears a coronet. By-and-
bye, we will accept our responsibilities. Mean-
while, Colston hangs and the Law in hanging
him commits, through sheer ignorance, a cold-
blooded assassination. — Wyong.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Old Cargo Boat.
The Australian sailor has no vote. Therefore,
he is of no earthly use to any member of
Parliament, and no member of Parliament cares
one single straw whether he is alive or dead,
and no Legislature troubles about his existence.
He is an unsatisfactory nomad, drifting hither
and thither on a troubled sea, and his life is of
no account to anybody at all. When he travels
on a passenger steamer he is in tolerable safety,
for the passenger steamer carries the politician,
and the politician's uncle, and other valuable
properties, and, therefore, it is subjected to a
regular half-yearly inspection by a Government
surveyor. But no politician ever travels by
the old, superannuated cargo steamer or sailing
hulk, and, therefore, that vessel is never in-
spected, and the only limit to its unspeakable
rottenness is the limit at which a crew
becomes unobtainable. The cargo boat is
generally a passenger vessel which has been
run into the last stage of dccrcptitudc,
and when the steitiner has become mangy
and crippled, and foundered on all its
extremities, it is just about fit for its new
career of usefulness. Then it is stripped of all
its life-saving apparatus, and every obstruction
which occupies space that might be otherwise
turned to account is removed and sold. The
oldest and most unsaleable boat is left for use
in conveying lines to the wharf, and one or two
life-buoys, which are frayed and busted and
unmarketable, remain on the bridge, to lend an
air of plausibility to the groaning hulk which
ploughs wearily through the heaving sea. The
boat will not hold one quarter of the crew,
and the alleged life-buoy will hold nothing
at all, but the insurance-policy, at least,
is sound and water-tight. Then the
aged craft probably enters into the coal-
trade, and plods surreptitiously by night
along the coast between Sydney and the
southern ports. She loads up during the day,
and her ponderous cargo is shot into her until
it towers high above her hatches, and her decks
are cumbered with coal, and there is coal every-
where from stem to stern. Then, in the dusk,
she coughs wearily away on her six or eight
hours’ run, and with the first gleam of dawn
she wheezes unobserved into Sydney Harbour,
and thus escapes the unpleasant comment
which she might otherwise arouse. This is
what happens if the weather is fine ; if it is not
fine, she runs dejectedly for the nearest shelter,
and if there is no shelter available she wallows
miserably like a waterlogged wreck amid
the waves. Then a few breakers roll over
her and pour into her Open hatchways,
and she goes down, and the owners collect
the insurance, and the public get up a relief
fund. Also the Marine Board finds upon
enquiry that the dead captain should have had
the hatches on, and no one mentions that if he
had the hatches on he couldn’t have carried
nearly so much coal, and if he hadn't carried
the coal he would, have been sacked. Also,
nobody takes any steps to insure that the live
captains, whose time for foundering hasn’t yet
come, have their hatches on, or that the owners
allow them to have them on. Further, it is
generally remarked that the dead and gone
vessel was deficient in live-saving apparatus,
but nobody does anything to the owners who
failed to supply the apparatus, or provides that
other cargo boats shall be better furnished in
this respect. For, as already mentioned, the
sailor is a nomad without a vote, and his fate is
nobody’s funeral but his own.
The other day the death of a Freetradc sup-
porter made a gap in the New South Wales
Assembly, and Pakkes shoved through a
pathetic resolution regretting “the loss which
this House has sustained ” by the decease of
Mr. Ritchie. Last year two Protectionist
members died and left two vacant seats in the
Legislature, and Pakkes didn't move worth a
cent that the House had sustained “ a loss
in their case. They were both men of some
note : Hakoed Stephen and James Feetcheu.
But Pakkes didn't even assume the outward
semblance of respect ■ to either of them.
Pakkes’ great ambition is to be mistaken for
Headstone, but he resembles him not even in his
collar. W hen Bea<'onseieed died, Gladstone
pronounced a funeral oration on the dead man
in the Commons; and Beaconsei eld, despite his
garish jewellery and the green velvet pants of
his younger days, would have done the same
had the positions reversed. So would almost
everybody else—except Pakkes. His grief
about Ritchie was only an election war-whoop.
The deceased member was about the dullest
politician in the House, and with his three
colleagues he made up the dullest, dreariest,
and most arid quartette in all New South W ales.
He was a decent, honourable man. but he was
not a loss in any political sense ; the House
wanted to lose him —not by death, but in any
other way that he could begot rid of. Pakkes’
hollow' note of melancholy imposed on nobody.
It was all empty. He had got measured for his
grief, and the thing was made to order.

Among the half-dozen or so of banks and alleged
banks which have burst mysteriously witlun
the last few weeks in various parts of Australia
there is one characteristic which seems to turn
up every time—the reserve fund can't be found.
Every bank that breaks has a reserve fund, but
it always seems to be kept in some place where
no one can get at it when it is w anted. Most
of the defunct institutions paid a big dividend
at last balance, and carried a large sum to the
said “reserve,” but either they forgot the
bearings of the place they carried it to, or else
the road is too dreadfully uphill for it to be
carried back again, for it never seems to get
back in rime to do any service. Why they all
keep these funds in such an inaccessible spot
has never been explained. A very small amount
hidden in a pair of old boots under the counter
would have saved several institutions which
have come to grief with a gigantic amount of
“ undivided profits ” glaring out of the balance-
sheet, but it never happened to be there. The
whole banking system seems to want revision.
There ought to be a black-tracker in every
bank to get on the trail of the protit-and-loss
account when it is wanted ; or else the reserve
fund should be abolished altogether, and the
general manager should simply keep a £lO,OOO
Government debenture concealed in his ear tor
use in emergencies. The present scheme is of
no earthly good.
“Hanging Judge” Hawkins : “Whether

all in this country (England) is matter for the
gravest doubt.” From such a quarter, this is
satisfactory.
The usual board has been appointed by
the Victorian Government to apportion the reward
which was offered for the capture of Colston.
Presumably Colston himself won't get any of it.
though, to all outward appearance, he is entitled
to the whole lot. The way ho was “captured”
was very simple: he walked into a public-house
and explained to a man who was standing at the
bar that he was the famous murderer, and was
ready to be run in. Then the man ran for a
policeman, and had to bring him along by mam
force, because the officer persisted in regarding
the whole affair as an idiotic hoax. The only
person who did any capturing was Colston him-
self, for he had actually to go out and catch a
citizen and explain matters to him before he
could get himself comfortably arrested. 1 ’rider
these conditions, where does the reward come in ?

-‘>h Dear Moorabinda, —
The Jerseys seem to have been carrying on rather un-
accountably during their sojourn in the back regions. At
the Broken Hill festivities, for instance, Lord Jersey
amiably ukased that full dress wasn’t a crying necessity,
as a man who is worth meeting in his Sunday clothes is
just as well worth meeting in moleskin pants, &c. (alas !
for the dress-coat procession up Bridge-street in the hot
noontide on “levvy” days!) while Lady Jersey wished it
to he understood that at her receptions no style of dress
would be de rigueur 1 Now, this was quite too sweet of
M. E. Jersey, of course, but altogether superfluous, don’t
,rou think? For instance, when has the vice-regal lady
mandated what clothes her she-subjects are to put on
when they “pay their respects to M’Guinness?” The
public Governor publicly orders “frills on” for his
levees because man is a non-dressy animal who would
turn up in a flannel shirt at a wedding if he wasn’t closely
watchcd, but woman is altogether different, and can be
relied upon to wear her entire wardrobe whenever occa-
sion offers. The Broken Hill lady is probably as nicely
arrayed as lots of the women who crOw'd public receptions
in Sydney (I have in my mind’s eye whole miles of shabby
frocks, rainbow colours, and burnt straw hats) and if she
is dressed any worse it is only another phase of aesthetic
suffering of the vice-regal lady—that is all. Probably the
female population of Broken Hill is now seized with the
notion that Sydneyites turn up in feathers and bare
shoulders and carriages at Lady J.'s social afternoons !
they have perhaps never seen the long file of weird
females in every possible variation of rag-bag finery
making mournfully up the drive to Govingment House.
Sydney lie-unions held their last hop but one at Niths-
dale on 18th inst., with Hardy at the piano and twelve
valseson a card of fifteen dances ! It seems a pity that
some people were not born teetotums. The dressing was
very pretty. A tall girl in heliotrope silk plainly made
with corsage cluster of violets ; Miss Benson, in white
Greek gown with bands of gold tinsel; a bank-manager’s
wife from Bathurst looked well in black, but another fair
girl, in black, had unwisely trimmed herself all down the
back of her skirt with natural wattle. No true Australian
likes to see our flora sat upon.
And this reminds me that Lady Jersey, in her latest
pomelets, speaks of the wattle in a joyous connexion.
Her Ex., it is plain, is still a new-chum. The wattle, as
Australia knows, is never associated with joy or cheerful,
ness, but only with death and misery—with the dingo
howling o’er a grave in the lonely bush and the midnight
mopehawk hooting by the black and foetid marsh.
There are palpable orthographical frills over other
names than Smijthe. “ Major W. D. Benny Tailyour” is
the label of the man appointed to succeed Col. De VVolski
as military grand panjandrum in N.S.W. Tailyour?—
Taylor 1 Mem. : Baker—Bakyour !

One of the most interesting sights on the evening of a
swell ball in Sydney is to see the carriages rolling up
with mamma and the girls inside and the old man shiver-
ing on the box next to the coachman. There is generally
plenty of interior space for papa if they squeezed up a
little, but, then, airy skirts mustn’t be scrunched, and so
he has to go aloft with only an alpaca top-coat between
his laundried buzzum and a howling westerly. Now r ,
isn’t it rather a G x 4 insight into the disposition of a
young woman when she sets her sire up in the teeth of
the gale, braving pneumonia in order that her tulle train
may have a seat to itself?
With a certain amount of trepidation I learn that the
newest fashion in dresses is that they will be made with a
form of waist-belt, or a girdle, encircling the figure a
degree lower than the waist. In short, the Bernhardtian
cestus is going to rage, and no matter how unfitted by
nature a woman may be to assume such a girthing,
nothing will stop her from hooping up like a barrel, even
though slip is fat and forty and outlined accordingly. A
belt a “dej(»fec lower than the waist” is a fearsome addi-
tion to any figure except the slim, straight one—Bern
hardt was too podgy to attempt one. When she thrust
heg jewelled thumbs into each side of that cestus and
turned her profile on to her adorers in front—well, some
people used to call it the “ cherubic,” which is after all
only the poetic phrase for something over-fed.

The third bride of a cracked Sydney solicitor (now
gathered to his fathers) who lived on a gloomy estate on
the shores of the South Pacific had a lively time of it
between the old man and the family vault wherein were
ranged her two predecessors. The vault was on the pre-
mises in a damp, dark spot shaded by cypress, and he
used to take her for a stroll to it after tea, and they
would perch on the ledge and chat about the dead women
underneath with the boom of the surf in their ears and
the sombre bush around them. It is needless to say that
the bride made another in the sarcophagus ere tire year
was out; anti probably found it quite cheerful.
Apropos of the Brisbane season with “Dr. Bill,’ Myra
Kemble tells of meeting a parson who pursed up a reprov-
ing lip on hearing that she was an actress. Nevertheless,
he begged her to go and hear him preach. “Willingly,”
said she, “ provided you will come and see me act.” And
he did, occupying a stage-box, and shrinking out of sight
of the audience - which didn’t suit Myra, who sent to say'
that unless he sat where the house could see him she
wouldn’t go to church. The good man obeyed the mis-
chievous command, and sat in the middle of the box,
w ith the result that Myra’s little brass button tinkled into
the collection-bag on the Saw bath.

battalions of combed-up infants were out manoeuvring in
the open with spades and watering-pots, though the local
board ladies did most of the planting, w hile the brats had

members of the Public School Board, headed by Minister
Carruthers and his wife. There is really no need to
specify what form the ceremonies took.

One thing always strikes me in the meteoric sassiely
actress, which is that the hint of the woman is always
stronger than that of the dressmaker. You never see a
tailor-built “star” buttoned up from neck to waist in
rigid tweed like a boy’s suit, no matter how modish it is to
resemble a fretwork figure, because there isn’t enough
scope in such a rig to cast a glamour over even the stage-
boxes. Half the “ charm ”of the beauteous creature lies
in the subordination of her drapery, which is made part
of her, revealing more than it conceals—hopelessly out of
fashion, of course, yet leaving the woman paramount.
And this is why the “society gal,” though she painfully
memorises airs and graces, cannot be quite so seductive
because her first agonised query' always is “ What is going
to be worn ? ”

arrangement ” is like a swell-barber’s dream of bliss.
From time to time I have longed to get into a tobacco-
concert ; I have resented the barriers of smoke which
interposed between melody and me, and I have pictured
all sorts of fun for the happy people inside. But a
sufferer tells me a sad tale. “ If,” said he, “the audience
were compelled to smoke the same brand of tobacco, these
concerts, saving that you can’t sec the platform for
smoke, would be well enough. It is true that the
singers nearly cough their heads off, and somebody
always strikes a wax match just as the tenor of the even-
ing is modulating his top A. But that’s nothing. It’s the
mix of a dozen brands of tobacco that makes you reach
for your hat.”
I have received, presumably from a lady, some of the
“Rita Curling Fluid’’—done up in a very professional
bottle, and accompanied by diagrams. But, after all,
cheap cold water acts just as well (with a little soap in
obstinate cases) as the fluid, which is merely to damp the
hair with ere torturing it round pins. What the female
community hungers for is a harmless decoction which
will frizzle their amaranthine bangs in one act.
No woman, say s a fashion paper (in effect), who cares a
pin about her appearance will ever go out without a veil.
Now, I calmly submit that veils are hampered by more
inartistic drawbacks than any other feminine fal-lal.
Watch a woman in a tulle fall getting through her lunch
at a cafe l . She must turn it up over her nose, when—
especially if it is dark-coloured—it gives an ugly divided
look to the prettiest face ; besides, it comes down at
inopportune moments and gets among the soup, and
when you have finished feeding and go to fix it in a kill-
ing way, there comes a crinkled grease-spot just on (he
tip of your nose, and the only alternative left you is to
take it off or go home. Also in paying calls a veil is
utterly aggravating. If you want to hypocritically kiss
your hostess’s last baby or sip your tea in deadly earnest,
that filmy rag has to be hauled up and disarranged, and,
as a result, you go away with a sort of beard of draggled
tulle where once was a crisp straight line. And I never
yet saw a woman who could hook her veil over her nose
with one hand and accept a cup of tea with the other
without looking eagerly greedy somehow.
110 is an irascible old gentleman at the best of times,
and he lives at Hand wick. Last Saturday night he took
his wife into town to hear the organ-recital, leaving his
16-year-old daughter and a young lady friend to mind
house. After the departure of the old people, the girls
started to make toffee, and there being no fire in the
kitchen, they brought their materials and so on into
the dining-room, and the ingredients were soon sizzling
merrily over the blaze. The molten toffee had just been
poured into a fiat pan when, horror, the latch-key was
heard in the front door, and the frightened girls had only
time to shove saucepan and toffee under the table, when
in marched pa and ma. They w ere amongst the thousands
turned away from the Town Hall. I’a, grumpier even
than usual, took off his boots and yelled for his slippers,
but, awaiting their arrival, he bad the ill-luck to draw up
to the table, and, putting his legs under the mahogany,
he plumped both feet into the boiling sugar. You should
have heard pa howl.

ful bugle, or that the journal in question has
mighty long ears. The ears have it, in our
opinion.
By cable from Maoriland we learn that
Moss, who attempted to murder his wife at
Ashburton amTtbcn tried to commit suicide, has
been found not guilty, on the ground of insanity
when the act was committed. This decision is
almost equivalent to a declaration that wilful
murder does not exist. No murderer can be
wholly in possession of his faculties at the actual
time of commission.
That genial old soul, Obed West, one of
Sydney’s patriarchs, is dead, aged 84, leaving
much money, land, and bricks-and-mortar. The
ancient undoubtedly knew a lot about the remote
past of Sydney, but was in the habit of mixing
his dates terribly, and confounding his personal
experiences with things which lie had only read.
He had never been out of N.S. Wales in all
his life.
South Australian Chief Justice Sammy
Way “who has been invited everywhere to meet
the German Emperor,” will, it is cabled, “spend
some days at High Wycombe in September help-
ing Lord Oarington to put the final touches to the
story of his Australian experiences.” Carington’s
“ experiences ” will be painfully incomplete unless
they contain a full account of the wormy crawl-
someness of some Australian lawyers.
Central Cumberland election should end
in the return of Labour Candidate Marshall.
The Bulletin, however, is rather surprised to
notice from the press reports, that Mr. Marshall
instead of sinking the fiscal question is inclined
to sound the praises of foreign trade and unduly
assault the Protectionist party. This course,
if persisted in, will certainly lead to the breaking
up of the N.S. W. Labour-party. The aggressive-
ness of the Single-tax apostles is a menace to the
solidity of the ranks.
“There is one thing, at least, to be said
about suicide : it puts an end to whatever trouble
one happens to be in—so far as we know ; and we
know all that can be known.” But the misery of
a clumsy attempt at suicide! Last week Mr.
Fred.' Brock, the well-known Sydney stock and
station agent, aged 49, shot himself with a pistol.
The bullet entered the right temple, fracturing
the cheek bone, passing through the right eye,
and coming out of the left eye. The sight of both
eyes has been destroyed. At time of writing the
unfortunate man was lying in the hospital in a
hopeless state. Mrs. Brock is at present absent
on a visit to a relative at Norfolk Island.
Premier Parkes, in his now Electoral Bill,
lias named one of the Sydney Divisions after
Doctor Bland, the friend and colleague of Went-
worth. Sad to relate, a son of the Doctor died a
couple of years ago in one of the public asylums
at Parramatta, lie was a man with a refined and
beautiful face, a clever portrait and animal painter
(horses and dogs were his forte), but a thorough
Bohemian. On the death of his mother at a great
age in England he succeeded to some money, which
from his habits only hastened the end. lie used
to say that his mother lived to such a great age
that she had seen “sets” of trustees under her
husband’s will pass avyay before her own time
came. • *

Money turned away from three parts of
the house on the first three nights of “Lights o’
London ” has doubtless caused George Rignold to
asseverate : “For this relief much tanks !” “The
Lights ’’ is not, viewed bye and large, a good play
for folks of dismal livers. It is pervaded by a
wrecked father, who has been such a callously-
oriminal scoundrel that it is difficult to ascertain
how he can consistently object to his ruined
daughter doing those tilings which she might
not have done had he shown her a better
example. It is also mottled throughout by
a gentleman arrayed in football costume and
a dirty filce, who drivels and whines about his
two-days’-empty stomach, in a fashion that is
calculated to evoke derision from those who have
seen the forty-days-fasting man. Perhaps, the
least supportable individual of all is the “pheno-
menon ” Shakespeare Jarvis, an irritating person
at any time, but simply insupportable when
trebled by a woman’s puny pipe. Still, there is a
deal of human natur’, some sound sense and heaps
of fun in “ The Lights,” and it is certainly good
fora big run, for as someone yelled from the “gods”
the other evening, when Sterling Whyte dropped
from the bridge into the stream and George
Rignold dived in after him “that’s worth the sugar
itself!” The drama is as -well played, if not
better, than heretofore. Perhaps, George Rig-
nold’s Harold Armytage is somewhat more
doughy than of old, but, nevertheless, the per-
formance is up to sample. Hairy Jewett, on the
other hand, is probably more finished, and in
Jarvis “Bill” Rignold has got somethng easy
and right into his hand. Mrs. Rignold’s Mrs.
Jarvis makes folks wonder why she so seldom
takes to the boards. James Cathcart reappears here
as Squire Armytage, after being absent since he
played Brutus in “ Julius Caesar.” Watt-Phillips’
Bess Marks is marked by the care and complete-
ness of all that lady’s work, but Bessie Rignold is
scarcely adequate to the frivolity expected from
Hetty Preene. The park-loafers of Frank Har-
court and Mabel Tracey, like the fat-faced grub-
stowing boy of Guyton Heath, are remarkably
olever little character sketches. An engine-band
snapped on Monday evening last and left the
house without the electric light for some minutes.

Revived, after a lapse of ten months,
“ A Night Off,” at Sydney Criterion, is likely to
do big business during the projected eleven nights
of its tenure. On Friday, Sept. 4th, this theatre
will be probably closed while the Brough contin-
gent are making for Melbourne, and on the
following night the Boucicaultian wing of this his-
trionic army will put up “ Peril,” recently played
in Melbourne. “Devotion,” Dot’s very own com-
position, probably follows. Marcus Brutus Snap,
played with a vacuous rotundity of voice that is
more Barry Sullivanish than Ansonian, and
looking like a drab pouter-pigeon, with a
red beak and rubescent hackles, is one
of William George Anson’s numerous best
performances, with all the latest embellishments
thrown in. For instance, speaking of Babbit’s
Roman tragedy, he says : “I II produce it if it’s
as bad as “Horny Hands and Bloody Noses.”
The cast has two curious resemblances to dead
heroes. _ George Gordon’s usher is a replica of the
“Hook” of Wellington; and Robert Brough’s
peevish, thin-voiced, watch-spring legged pro-
fessor might pass fairly well about the head for
Abe Lincoln just escaped from Major Dane’s
lecture-list. Miss Romer, Annie Taylor—some-
what too gushy—“ Ted ” Corlessc, Cecil Ward
and Emma Temple repeat former successes, but
Pattie Browne’s Nisbe is a long way from being
good ; and, as Damask, Frank Cates was never
seen to Jess advantage. On first night the show
flagged slightly here and there, but, urged by
cyclonic outbursts of cachinuation, now goes like
hot pies down the throat of a nigger-minstrel.
35 * 35 *
Elton’s Mayor, who is also an “ old
guard,” is a chameleonic individual; the other
night he reminded us strongly of Toole’s Caleb
Plummer, then later of Maccabe’s “fascinating
fellow ” shorn of his whiskers. His gags over the
broken bayonet of “made in Germany,” and
“ raining in England, doncherknow,” as he turns
up the hems of his tight pants, are received with
such howls of delight nightly, that The Bulletin
inclines to think there would be money in hash-
ing together all the popular sayings, serving them
up with popular airs and dishing them as a plotless
comic opera. There would be absolutely no
strain on the listener’s mind, little on the actor’s,
possibly none on that of the treasurer. By the
way, “ King Billy’s ” exquisite fooling at the end
of the second act kills a rather pretty sextette
stone-dead.

John Roberts, for about 18 years the un-
disputed champion of all-round British billiards
—nrince of the “push,” in fact —concluded a
season, which became highly successful with the
lowering of prices, at the Crystal Palace Hall,
Sydney, on Tuesday. Roberts, the “iron man ”
of billiards, is as square, as hard-looking,
physically and mentally, as on his prior visit, and
is,” perhaps, even more impassible, for any
measure of impetuosity that he may have once
possessed seems to have been eradicated by a
long course of public appearances, and the steely-
grey eyes now light up an organism apparently
as chilly as that of a cold-water eel.
They say Roberts plays better than he did ; the
improvement was not manifest to a merely casual
dropper-in, who missed the dash and daring that
made “young John,” the hero of the British
billiard world, when he and Cook with “Joe”
Bennett, Timbrell and “Master” Stanley were
revolutionising the “ royal game.” But the quick-
sighted , decisive directness, the unerring j udgment,
the cool imperturbability, the old determination
and confidence, the touch, delicate as a lady’s,
allied to the wrist-power of a blacksmith, all
remain, and as the experience is, of course, en-
larged, it is probable that Roberts does play
a better game now than at any prior period
of his career. In Sydney he easily defeated J ames,
Birkett, Parker, and Weston, who received starts
from one-half to six-tenths the game. Roberts’
largest break, 437, was made with Birkett, and
Weston’s 94 secured for that player a silver cup.
The afternoon performances have been invari-
ably attended by many ladies, and the guber-
natorial party has put in several appearances.
Roberts now'plays through the country districts
of N.S. Wales towards Queensland, then visits
Adelaide and Broken Hill prior to touring Maori-
land.
#* * *
Times have changed. The Age, in a lead-
ing article on the MacMahonian drama, says that
Slugger Sullivan “commends himself even as a
physical curiosity to neither gods nor men, and
only seems a rather stupid caricature of what he
really is.” But which, by the way, is not correct.
Sullivan upon the stage looks exactly what he is—
a retired prize-fighter run to fat.
** * *
Although “ II Trovatore ” and “ Martha ”
outstayed their welcome at Melbourne Alexandra,
the shilling pit, which may be accepted as the
barometer of an Italian opera season, has remained
at “ set fair ” thus far, wherefore Madame Simon-
sen may reckon upon getting through with a
credit balance if she makes haste to put novelties,
positive or comparative, before an evidently
appreciative public. “Un Ballo in Maschera”-
that favourite feast of melodies—woke things
right up on Monday, and will keep the ballo
rolling this (Thursday) and following night. The
one young and sylphlike lady of the company,
Signorini Mattioli, blooms for the first time in this
production as Oscar, page to the giddy governor
who ultimately comes to grief through singing
illicit duets with his secretary’s wife. Winsome
Mattioli lends a delicate flavour of leg-show to
an otherwise tragic story of intrigue and political
upheaval. The strength and weakness of the
present Italian invasion have been fairly sampled
by this time. Cuttica is a delightfully sympa-
thetic, yet powerful tenor, whilst Kilalta meets
ordinary requirements as a robusto, but for stage
love-making—the business of all tenors since time
was—the one is too short and chubby, and the
other too small and wooden. Both the baritones,
however, are much more satisfactory in a pictorial
sense, and Melossi has a noble voice entirely. The
fair primi-donni, buxom and matronly-looking,
will always pass muster as actresses in heavy
operas, and compel admiration as vocalists.
Cavalleri, who was formerly cast for all the lead-
ing contralto parts, is now able to take turn and
turn about with an efficient colleague inSignorina
Castiglioni; the basso department is fairly well
looked after, and, with orchestra and chorus in
capital working order, the entertainment, taking
one consideration with another, may be summed
up as the best of the three produced under Simon-
sen management.
* *■ * *
“Devotion,” a short three-act Frenchy
drama from the pen of Dion Boucicault, jr., was
elegantly staged at the Bijou on Saturday night,
when His Excellency graciously consented to take
the chair. Described as original, save in the
matter of one leading incident, “Devotion” is a
decidedly artificial story of the Richelieu period,
and the plot is so completely dominated by the
great Cardinal and his royal master that their
absence from the cast is a matter for regret. The
players appear as mere puppets moved to hopeful-
ness or despair, by someone who is pulling a
string behind the scenes. That the ph*y is fa rly
well written and constructed goes without saying,
inasmuch as the author is a Boucicault all over,
and an expert in stage work. But “ Devotion ’is
not a good play, all the same. The hero and
heroine arouse no interest in their troubles,
and don’t appear to be quite certain
whether they are giddy or virtuous. Several
other people, including the heroine’s husband,
ai’e too much mixed up with events that transpire
elsewhere, and there is no air of reality about
things that would, perhaps, appear exciting in
print. For this fatal shortcoming Percy Lyndal
and Mrs. Brough are partially responsible. The
lady occasionally makes a bold attempt to rise to
a false situation, although hampered by a
clumsily-out train; but Lyndal is as unimpressive
as his silk pyjamas, which, by the way, ought to
be frilled and fussed out below the knee.
Titheradge is less happy than usual as a rather
stupid sort of husband, and author Boucicault
strikes one as being the youngest Abbe that ever
went on the loose in holy orders.
** * *
J. L. Sullivan drama is considerably
patronised by football barrackers, and other male
members of the unintelligent classes at Melbourne
Opera House, but the rational public seem to have
had enough of a deplorable spectacle on the open-
ing night. Since then the star performer has
turned up in a solemn, sober condition, and
as he sullenly ignores all gallery invitations to
“ put a tail ” on the rest of the company, there is
nothing new to be said concerning that remark-
able man.
** * *
“Cinder Ellen” drew a seething first-
night crowd to Melbourne Princess’ on Saturday
last, and seems likely to satisfy the followers of
Gaiety burlesque for the remaining three weeks of
its stay. Whether this brand-new absurdity (said
to be the work of Fred. Leslie) is better or worse
than “Ruy Bias ” is a question that could only be
satisfactorily settled by a jury of Johnnies,
capable of drawing nice distinctions between the
idiotic and the inane. Considering that the same
people play the fool, dance, sing, and continually
change their clothes in either “burlesque,” with
the same old Gaiety regard for a proper distribu-
tion of “fat” in both instances, it is hard for a
tired journalist to see the necessity for ever
altering the names of these variety shows. All
that is done in “Cinder Ellen” might have been
done in “Huy Bias,” or vice versa, without hurt-
ing anybody’s feelings, or making matters more
incoherent than at present. No thread of
humour would be snapped if half of the one
were taken away and sandwiched between two
quarters of the other. The public, who are woe-
fully apt to lose a thread of humour even when
they get it, would probably swallow greedily a
stew composed of odd bits from ten burlesques,
provided that the usual quantity of legs and
topical allusions were thrown into the pot to
represent onions. The performers make what-
ever fun can be discovered in “ Oinder-Ellen.”
Sometimes they are more funny than a dead
rhinoceros. They sink to their lowest depth of
humour in a pantomimic episode which concludes
with a chorus—“ The monn has got his trousers
on.” If Fred. Leslie designed, arranged, and in-
vented this last-mentioned item, he can’t be such
a clever man as one might think. In his stage
character, however, Frederick is bright, mercurial
and amusing as ever. Ditto Cinder-Ellen Farren.
Grace Pedley’s ballad singing and the dances by
Sylvia Grey and Florence Levey are exactly what
was expected, but in point of magnificence the
show falls short of one’s wildest dreams. Less
expensive entertainments have seemed more
gorgeous to the general.
* *- * *
Miss Laura Yilliers and Co. are still con-
fronted by more bare bench than audience at the
old Royal. On Saturday the lady from London
produced “ A Celebrated Case,” without mention-
ing on the playbill that it is just about identical
with Burnand's adaptation of “ITn Cause Celebre”
played in England under the name of “Proof.”
The original run of “ Proof,” one of the strongest
of dramas in capable hands, was remarkable
for the fact that four actors —Charles Kelly,
Bandmann, Harry Neville, and Hermann Vezin
played the hero in rapid succession. The writer
saw three of them in the part within about three
months. Others of a splendid company were
Louise Moody, Arthur Stirling, Mrs. Bandmann,
Charles Harcourt, Bella Pateman, and Mrs.
Billington. With such people in the mind’s eye,
it is impossible to say very nice things about the
very mediocre Yilliers company, but they do their
best, and the drama is quite strong enough to
enable them to make a success.
** * *
Two years ago Snazelle opened his one-
man show at Melbourne Athenaeum before a
handful of payers and the rest deadheads. On
Saturday, when that mariner bold re-opened the
same proceedings, the public came rolling up the
hill, like Bonyparty at the battle of Waterloo, to
hear an established favourite give an entertain-
ment which is now understood to be first-rate in
several different ways. The music-halls are all
right, and the Eureka Stockade picture has just
been fixed up at the Cycldrama.
** * *
A well-known pug. is just now a perfect
terror to the waiters at one of the chief Melbourne
hotels. There it is by no means uncommon to find
a waiter with his head screwed round the wrong
way, a bruised arm, and a broken spirit, as the
result of his temerity in venturing into the
slugger’s private room with a supply of spirituous
liquors.

Fanny Enson, whose Countess Zicka in
“ Diplomacy,” and Louise in “Frou Frou” will
be remembered while memory hold its sway,
leaves for England shortly. She takes a farewell
benefit at Sydney Criterion on Wed., Sept. 9,
when a mighty multitude will wdthout doubt
assemble to bid a universal favourite farewell.
At Poverty Point.
According to a Sydney paper Sara Bernhardt was
“caressed by many friends” on gaining the stateroom
which the Mariposa’s captain had relinquished in her
favour. For “ many friends” read “certain critics” and
you hit the gusher on a soft spot.
Pattie Browne's knee-cap wasn’t really fractured ; only
strained, through early piety, presumably.
Highflyer Higgins was dashed to death in England,
aeronaut Annie Harkess fell hard from a great height at
Oincinnatti (U.S.) and succumbed to the hardness of the
ground, parachutist Pieroni dived from a great altitude
into the Adriatic and became food for fishes, and balloonist
Buckingham found no footing on the waters of Lake
Syracuse (U.S.); further, the foolhardy Fernandez, of
Australia, having avoided death by drowning, was nearly
grilled to death while entangled|by the ropes of his burn-
ing balloon in a Queensland tree-top. Curiously enough,
contemporaneously with these disasters to air-navigators,
comes news of improvements to Maxim’s flying machine—
a steam-kite actually—which, propelled through the air
by a light screw, can, it is cabled, carry a weight of two
tons at a speed of 90 miles an hour.
musical “ classicists ” of Sydney are nearly insane
at the tremendous success of Organist Wiegand’s popular
recitals at Centennial Hall. The huge building has been
absolutely packed every time.
Peace is, we hear, restored at London Lyceum, and
Henry and Ellen are playing together in “Ravenswood.”
All the same, she will not accompany him to America
whenever he goes on pleasure bent. She prefers her
villegiatura at a quiet watering-place in England.
“ The Nautch Girl,” writes our London correspondent
“ thanks to the old stagers like Denny, is gradually work-
ing up into a good opera. The bookings are enormous,
almost up to the highest standard of the old Carte-Sulli-
van-Gilbert regime.
Haddon Chambers says that the play he is now writing
for Tom Tlmrne will set the seal on his fame. His fees
for “The Idler ” amount to £75 per week.
Christie Murray is back in London, looking well. He is
determined to test the judgment of the London critics on
his dramatic work, and has nothing but what is good to
say of the “ Colonics.” No sooner had he set foot in
England than he was met with matrimonial troubles. He
is not quite sure whether or not he will venture on the
boards—at least for a while.
M. Maurel, the French baritone, recently distinguished
himself at the Actors’ Benevolent Fund dinner, in London,
He accepted a treble encore for his song and gave £lOO to
the Fund in return.
A London County Councillor has dropped £250 damages
and costs for slandering a marionette performance at
Westminster Aquarium. He declared that he saw through
his spectacles, one marionette behave indecently towards
another marionette, whereas both figures were proved to
be as pure and chaste as a Baptist congregation, or more
so. The old gentleman, it appears, wears blue spectacles
and naturally discovers a cerulean meaning in every-
thing.
Our old friend, Professor Sample, in now engaged in
taming horses by machinery at Hengler’s Circus. The
process is described as simple, and of a bank-holiday
characters. Sample fixes up his fiery steeds in a sort of
steam round-about, from which they presently emerge
with humble and contrite hearts.
