The American soprano who took up Nellie Stewart
rejected part in “The Nautch Girl” turned out a dire
failure. How distressed must poor Nellie feel at this 1
Madame Simonsen advertises “ Martha ” in preference
to “ Marta.” It is, in truth, absurd to Italianise the name
of the heroine of an opera which includes such sternly
English characters as Nancy and Plunket. You can’t
make them into Italians, nohow or anyway. If memory
tells truly, “Martha” was Italianised in the interests of
Giugluini, the tenor of 30 years ago.
Arthur Dacre and his talented wife (Amy Roselle) have
been having a terribly bad time in America. B. &B.
ought to pick (hem up cheap for a season in Australia
where the lady, at any rate, would speedily take the
shine out of one of her “star” imitators. Dacre, too, is a
nice-looking actor of superior Frank Cates quality, and
the pair might do almost as well as the Kendals, who
seem to have left no room for them in Yankeeland.
Beerbohm Tree intends to make his Hamlet dehut
before a Scotch or Irish audience.
Irving is adopting all sorts of strange devices to keep
business brisk at the Lyceum. He gives morning per-
formances, in the goody-goody interests, ever}’ Wednes-
day and Saturday, and shuts up shop for the rest of those
days. Fancy an Australian manager closing his show on
Saturday nights !
The theatrical birds will mate in spring. The lovely
leading man at the other Sydney comedy-house is about
to wed also ; but he steps off the stage for his wife.
Tilly Salinger, the plump, is still doing _ well in’Frisco,
where they’ve also taken kindly to her sister, Lena, the
leaner one.
The play-without-permission-using barnstormers have
escaped owing to a flaw' in the indictment; the pursuer
having applied for £5O damages when the law only
allowed £lO.
Ex-Bank-manager Bradley, brought from prison to
testify in the Insolvent Court, deposed that he spent from
£20,000 to £25,000 of the Bank’s money in his skating-
rink business.
The elder Silbon died recently on the voyage from
South America, through worry caused by losses in that
country.
Myra Kemble goes to Queensland in September with
“Dr. Bill” and “Jane,” then, probably, to Maoriland.
St. Clair is business manager.
Charles Westmacott, one of our most promising actors,
a white man in every way and a thoroughly good and
genial fellow, sailed from Sydney for London in the
Murrumbidgee on Saturday morning. He may return
soon; meantime, he will be best remembered for his
auctioneer in “The Octoroon,” Tybalt in “ Romeo and
Juliet,” and his old dodderer in “The Middleman” —
three quite dissimilar characters.
Fred. Neebe takes a Sydney benefit soon.
Marie Frazer, a lady w’ho, if memory does not fall, recently
made a London hit in one of Ibsen’s plays, has arrived
in the Oruba under engagement to B. and B. She opens
in “Sunlight and Shadow,” one of several new' plays
secured by Alex Mayne for the firm. By the w*ay, he has
also engaged Miss James, a smart soubrette, with an
Adelphi reputation, and has secured tor B. and B. the
first Australian refusal of every play Pinero, Grundy and
Jerome write for the English stage.
Mr. R. B. Wiseman, a well-known man in theatrical
circles, has now been pronounced incurably blind. He
takes a benefit at Her Majesty’s on Sept. when
selections from “ Lights o’ London,” “ Old Guard,” “ Dr.
Bill,” and “The Idler,” will be played by the companies
of the four Sydney theatres now occupied. Musical and
other items will he contributed by unattached members
of the profession.
Fred Millis, the Australian gastriloquist, is show ing at
London Metropolitan. He gets great jam from the
papers.

moments. “Ah, Wybert, my boy, just put a cheroot in
my mouth and light it. Thanks, thanks.” By the way,
Reeve says Mathews smoked the very worst cigars.
“Oneof Greville’s cleverest things was Jonathan Wild, in
a “Jack Shepherd” burlesque, and truly the audience
were knocked out of time by his extraordinary disguises,
the last one being that of. leader of the orchestra, from
which position he leaped on the stage. Leslie had better
introduce it as new*.
Brian Dailey was recently met in one of the London
clubs, very much the West End swell. His voice had been
restored to him by a surgical operation, and altogether
Brian was the Brian of old, when Sydney ladies raved
over him and the morning post brought him many
delicately-scented notes.
Catherine Sinclair, widow of Edwin Forrest, died a few’
weeks ago in America, aged 76. She acted in Sydney and
Melbourne 36 years ago with Henry Sedley in “ Much Ado
About Nothing” and other pieces. She was a very
beautiful woman. Forrest, who married her in England,
falsely accused her, and she got a divorce with £3OO
allowance a year, but Forrest never paid it. She died
poor as an actress.
Alfred Byron, a son of playwright H. J. Byron and
member of'the North West (Winnipeg) mounted police,
either suicided or was murdered in his room recently.
Strauss has just celebrated the 21st anniversary of the
writing cf his “ Blue Danube.”
Ovide Musin, the French violinist, is coming to Aus-
tralia.
Clifford Harrison, the popular English monologuist,
visits Australia next year.
David Lee, who lately lost his wife, will, it is rumoured,
shortly resign his position as Melbourne city organist.
Madame Marian Burton goes home in the Anstralien.
She received a cheque for 300 guineas from (lie Halles for
breach of contract.
S. V. Winter, who lately resigned the editorship of
Melbourne Herald, was notorious for his indifference to
the blandishments of theatrical managers, among whom
lie was generally known as “ the Winter of our dis-
content.”
Tennyson, having long supplied grease to
the British Royal family, has now gone into the
dairying business. Thus Ashby Sterry :
I’m grieved to hear—to bear 'tis hard—
It puts me in a flutter, man—
Lord Tennyson, our matchless bard,
lias just beco.uie a butter-man !
No longer bo's content to glide
Along poetic, silky way ;
But turns bis Pegasus aside,
To gallop in the milky way !
The secretary of the N.S.W. “National
Association is secretary of the Employers’ Union.
Well, that’s an honest and straightforward
coincidence, anyway,
Archibald Forbes in the Eclectic Maga-
zine, takes the ground that in coming wars the
attacking armies are nearly certain to be repulsed,
armies acting on the defensive being almost in-
vincible because of the effectiveness of. rapid-
firing guns.
The trouble with prohibition is that the
states which adopt it, like Maine, lowa, and
Kansas, can never get enough of it. The more
stringent their laws the louder the everlasting cry
for amendments. — Mukat Halstead.
Robert Charles Cole, recently sentenced
to five years’ penal servitude for defrauding the
Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, has
left an interesting confession in the hands of his
solicitor. When the contents of that document
see the light of day more than one young man will
squirm.
A Manchester.(Eng.) man, John Smith,
angered at his name so frequently figuring in the
criminal records and other awkward places (no
John Smith was ever illustrious), became abnor-
mally apprehensive lest he should be confused
with one of these bad John Smiths, so he deter-
mined to assume a name like unto no other borne
by mortal man—Gagadig Gigadab. Bet you can’t
say it after three drinks.
The Salvation Army issues the biography
of Maggie Donohue. Twenty years ago she mur-
dered another woman with an axe in Punch’s
Lane, Little Bourke-street, Melbourne, was sen-
tenced to death, and then imprisoned for life;
served 15 years, was liberated, and taken up by
the Salvation Army; made a useful sergeant at
the Rescue Home, died a few months ago “be-
loved and respected by all.”
It was originally intended that Emperor
William should visit the London Naval Exhibi-
tion on a Sunday. But when a large, tallow-faced
M.P. fixed Mr. Speaker with his fishy eye, and
asked him whether he thought the Board would
like one of the Anointed to break the laws of
Moses, the diplomatic William telegraphed over
from Germany that he would “ do ” the show on a
week day. So the Sunday was set apart for a
select royal orgie, and Christianity went on as
usual.
Says a Bulletin London correspondent:—
It is a subject of remark that the German Emperor
speaks English with much less of a foreign accent
than his uncle, Albert Edward. In point of fact,
the Kaiser’s English is almost perfect. A great deal of
rubbish has been written in the Telegraph and other
papers respecting the Emperor’s personal appearance.
“ Every inch a king,” say they. In reality he is a ven
ordinary-looking person, with nothing commanding at all
about him. He is short, walks badly, despite his long
military training, and has a pale, almost sallow com-
plexion. His forehead is high and intellectual, and his
smile is winning. He has a certain hauteur of manner,
too, which is, in its way, effective, especially w hen he is
compelled to listen to dreary and frequently ill-worded
addresses. On the whole, he was very well received in
England, though it can hardly he said that his presence
evoked the same enthusiasm as did his fathers. One of
the features of the procession to the Guildhall was the
cheering when the Prince of Wales’ carriage passed.
Much of this, however, was undoubtedly for the Princess,
but Albert Edward took it all to himself apparently, if
one may judge by the gracious manner in which he
bowed to the crowd.
Mr. Thomas Taylor, Foreign-trade candi-
date for Central Cumberland (N.S.W.), objects to
Mr. Garrard’s candidature for the same place,
because “ it is a shame to import a gentleman to
crush out local ability.” This may be good logic,
but it is woefully bad Ereetrade. The only use of
Ereetrade is to import cheap foreign articles to
crush out local ability, and the one essential is
that they must be cheap. Garrard is cheap, and
the only thing Taylor can do to excuse his
existence is to be cheaper than Garrard. As
Taylor is a cheap man by nature he may succeed,
but even then he shouldn’t complain of Garrard,
for the essence of both their creeds is free com-
petition, and if six men after one seat isn’t free
competition, then what is?
They have a kind and thoughtful constable
at Outram (M.L.). He was prosecuting a local
landlord for Sunday-trading, and had brought in
two witnesses to testify, when he discovered, to
his alarm, that they were both very drunk. It
was a tight place, but in the bright lexicon of the
“ cop.” there was no such word as fail. Seizing
the drunker of the two, he rushed him out into the
yard, and poured twelve buckets of water over
him to bring him to. Then he rushed him back
again just as his name was called, and the testifier
lurched into the box with water pouring out of his
hair and his ears, and half-a-bucket in each
pocket, and a lot more in his hat, and stood there
coughing and blowing his nose violently. Then,
in a fatal burst of confidence, he addressed the
bench as “ old rooster,” and the case fell through.
The wet drunk was committed for contempt, and
his companion, the dry drunk, was left on the
pavement, and the proceedings terminated.
“ Universalist ” thus addresses us :
Dear Bulletin,—l noticed in your last issue a para-
graph wherein you set out that O’Sullivan, M.P.,
opposes divorce on behalf of the section whose religion
denies them the moral, social and political right of
divorce (?) I beg to inform you that the R.C. Church is
not a civil or political institution. Catholics care very
little what Divorce Bills are passed; such legislation in
noway affects us. In the R.C. Church matrimony is a
sacrament, and quite as obligatory as the other sacra-
ments of that Church. It is not merely a. contract
entered into between two persons who seek the
pleasures of conjugal love. Marriage has a very dignified
definition in the R.C. Church. In the R.C. religion
there is no doctrine favouring divorce, and if such were
inserted, “to keep up with the times, you know,” the
religion would cease to be the same as it has been since
its inception, 1900 years ago, to the present day. The
R.C. Church exists in all “political climes,” and has
never altered its doctrines to suit anybody. Catholics
hold that a sacrament is altogether outside politics.
Here comes the “ rub.” You are always preaching
against religious interference in politics; why, then, do
you favour political interference in religion ? We have a
religion and are satisfied with it, so you need not go into
hvst»ria over our “ misfortune." Let those who want
the “moral,” “social” and political right join some
other fold. Under the banner of the Roman Catholic
Church, women are, at least, free from the designs of
depraved men.

POLITICAL POINTS
There are but six Labour representatives
in the Commons.
The Victorian Legislative Council appears
to be entirely under the political control of three
firms of solicitors.
-A Tasmanian M.L.C.-the other day rose
to a point of order. His point was that the
speaker was leaving out all his aitehes.
Bir George Grey’s law reform measure,
which opens the profession to women, has passed
the Maoriland House of Representatives.
Parkes says he is not responsible for any
“itch in the N.S.W. Electoral Bill.” Neverthe-
less, he will be compelled to scratch much of it
out.
The violence of the Age attacks upon
Commissioner Speight is making people look for
some virtue in the Victorian Railways management
after all.
Has Boss Templar Clarke, M.L.A., in
accordance with the “Labour edict,” one-man-
one-billet, resigned the paste-pot and scissors of
the Temperance Would ?
Schnadhorst, the father of the “ Caucus,”
and former lackey to Joe Chamberlain, has apolo-
gised for having presented the brother of that
right honourable renegade to a radical consti-
tuency.
Parkes’ admiration for Crick is marked.
He beams with delight whenever that smart young
man takes the floor. As a matter of fact, Crick
says all the nasty things that nothing but Parkes’
position keeps him from saying.
Toby Barton should know better than to
say a Governor’s speech is “a speecli from the
throne.” But he was a member of the National
Australian Convention which wanted to compel
the Australias to Federate “under the Crown.”
The supersensitiveness of a few Labour-
members in the N.S.W. Assembly to political
badinage is becoming rather ridiculous. The
philosophy of the thing, however, seems to be that
no man is so easily offended as lie who is in deadly
earnest.
Mr. Chanter, ALL. A. (N S.W.), has a
sneaking liking for the plural-vote system, as he
holds that people who have property in N.S.W.,
but who, for commercial reasons, do not live in
the province, should bo allowed to exercise the
franchise.
The true cause of the weakening of
Parnell's influence in Ireland arises from the fact
that, for the time, his work is done. Irish dis-
satisfaction was built up on tenant troubles and
the Land Purchase Bill has momentarily quieted
agrarian agitation.
Ex-Premier Murehead (Q,), who hastened
out from England to save his beloved fatherland
from the wicked shearers, has duly come to hand.
He thinks Australian Federation can only be
brought about by “ outside pressure, such as a
war. ’ \\ ith whom- —and for what?
The American Consul in Melbourne says
that the legal machinery of Victoria (and N.S.W.)
is clumsy and absurdly expensive as compared
with that of the United States. He might, too,
h.i'e instanced Canada, where one can drop into a
lawyer's office and get quite a Jot of work done in
less than no time for a modest four or live dollars.
It is as good as settled that Duncan Gillies
will take over the Vie. Agent-Generalship from
(i raham Berry early next year. Duncan has made
his pile, Ids political light burns lower with each
fresh railway “ revelation,” and it’s about time
that lie went “Home” and became “Sir”
1 hmcan.

The method of manufacturing votes on
[•oiling day in West Sydney is thus described by
Jawbone Neild ; -“ A building is hired, and it is
fitted up with a large number of spare coats, wigs,
and false whiskers, and people get Is. apiece for
going to the polling place, and voting in the names
of other people. Some men get3os. a day by com-
mitting this offence against the laws of God and
man.”

delivered himself of this ex-cathedra opinion in
the N.S.W. Assembly
lam delighted to see a clause in the Bill which requires
that it a man cannot write he shall not have a vote. I
no not want illiterate foreigners coming to this country
and swamping our educated Australians.
Pretty cool from a barrister whose university
polish was presumably paid for with the proceeds
ot a State pension.
It has just been pointed out that secular
education is omitted from the N.S.W. Labour
Electoral League platform. The Bulletin pre-
sumes that this is due to the fact that the educa-
tion question is regarded as settled, so far as its
,®i® 18 concerned. One thing, however, is certain
Parkes’” education system is (unlike the
Victorian system) as yet neither free nor secular,
though it is supposed to be compulsory. To be
justly compulsory the system must bo made both
tree and secular.

New South Wales is notorious for the
number of its Royal Commissions. This political
disease is approaching alarming proportions.
The slightest opportunity is seized by Parke,s for
shunting his responsibility on to a commission,
composed chiefly of Parkesiau political dead-
beats. The Public Service Inquiry Board is the
laughing-stock of the Public Service : a quintette
of boys in knicker-bockers could not ask sillier
questions than they do; and for this frivolous
service handsome fees are paid. Hawthorne and
Abigail are now billeted on the country to
officially discover what everyone knows—the
gambling, immoral and insanitary habits of the
Chinese.
The delay of Parkes in appointing a Public
Works Committee is viewed with strong disfavour
by some members whose perquisites from this
source used to amount to several hundreds a year
in the last Parliament. The delay is excused on
the ground that £5,000,000 worth of public works
which have been approved of have yet to be
sanctioned by Parliament. But with the reten-
tion of this patronage in his hands the old man
feels he is in a better position to weather the
political storm. Were he sure of his present sup-
porters he would soon find a reason for bestowing
some fat billets among the members, and commis-
sioning them to report upon the expediency of
errecting, say a bridge to North Shore, a bridge to
Balmain, or a bridge to Glebe Point. Masterly
inactivity is surely Parkes’ card while the Elec-
toral Reform Bill is being knocked into shape.
The N.S. W. Assembly, on an off-night
last week, affirmed its opinion that all marble
required for public works should be obtained from
quarries owned and worked by the State. The
vote was virtually a censure of Bruce Smith’s
action in vetoing the use of colonial marble in the
new N.S.W. Lands Office. The principle, or
rather the extension of the principle, of State
Socialism was a live feature in the debate. The
emphatic vote of the House struck this minister
all of a heap; and while the members were
rubbing their hands with joy, and resuming their
seats, he smartly moved the adjournment of the
House—to avoid a second defeat on the question
that all rolling-stock required for the railways
should be manufactured in the country. To have
negatived his motion, as a minister, would have
meant the ministerial exit of Parkes with his
Some-men-one vote Bill.

At this moment the condition of the working pouula-

al laws, human and divine. The experience of his own
weakness urges men to call in help from without (work-

neb. 'Mien workpeople have recourse to astrike. it is
requently because the hours of labour are too long or
the work too hard, or because they consider the wanes
insufficient. The laws sliould be beforehand, and prevent
hose (roubles from arising. It is neither justice nor
humanity so to grind men down with excessive labour as
to stupefy their minds and wear out their bodies. Those
vviio labour in mines and quarries, and in work within the
newels of the earth, should have shorter hours in propor-
tion, as their labour is more severe and trying to health,
in regard to children, great care should be taken not to
place them in workshops or factories till their bodies and
minds are sufficiently mature. For just as rough weather
ties, troys the buds of spring, so too early an experience of
nre s hard work blights the young promise of a child’s
powers, and makes any real education impossible. Women
aie not suited to certain trades, for a woman is, by
nature, fitted for home work. If working people can lie
encouraged to look forward to obtaining a share in the
land the result will be that the gulf between vast wealth
and deep poverty will be bridged over.
Those members of N.S.W. Parliament
who, like the newly-elected Dr. Cullen, think it
good policy to resent the “insult to the press”
implied in the recent motion to establish in
.Sydney a daily Hansard, must be either grossly
ignorant or grossly hypocritical. The press
emphatically does not report, and never has
reported on their merits, the utterances of poli-
ticians. Anyone privileged to thoroughly know
the secrets of the prison-house is aware that in
every daily newspaper-office—the exceptions are
too few to vary the rule—a certain code of
instructions is from time to time issued by the
editor or the fat proprietor for the guidance of Par-
liamentary reporters. Where is the scribe who
lias not in the course of his experience
seen the F.P. enter the reporter’s room with
Aever let the name of So-and-So appear in
m y paper to the extent of more than two lines
unless it is in connection with a criminal charge
preferred against him and then bump it out or
something like that ? Yet the proscribed M.P.
in such eases, often is one of the cleverest men and
best worth listening to in the whole country- but
lie has committed the unpardonable crime of act-
ing independently and sticking his knife into the
tat proprietor’s private interests. For years there
were standing instructions in Melbourne daily
newspaper offices to boycott David Gannsonjust
as there were for many years standing instruc-
tions, executed to the letter, in Sydney offices, to
boycott and stifle Melville and Luscombe, and
every other lone-hand N.S.W. member who did
not join in the chorus of the song which it pleased
a conspiracy to sing. “Cut ’em off with a line ”
was the word in the Parliamentary gallerv. So
little dependence is to be placed on newspaper
reports that in the absence of the official short-
hand-writers you could in after years pin no poli-
tician to anything under Heaven. Premier Parkes
says he “ would vote for the abolition of the
official reporters of the house to-morrow.” Very
likely. The official reporter is the man who acts
as memory to the Parliamentary turner and
twister. He is the man who holds the wriggling
eel by the back of its neck with a firm grip.

Personal Items.
Sir William Macleay has long been in ill-
hcalth.
What on earth would Farkes look like in
a ’ard straw ’at with a blue ribbon round it ?
Ban. O’Connor’s aptitude for blarney is
now world-famous. It even gets a paragraph in
the Family Herald.
Durant Harcourt, the journalistic attorney,
who died in Melbourne lately, got his fatal illness
through being garrotted.
The Sydney dailies are now getting nasty
over Lady Jersey’s poetry. They call the latest
sample “unpretentious.”
London paper: 880 million of prayers
have been offered, in church, for H.R.H., and the
only result is Tranby Croft.
When the mail left, Mr. Charles Potter,
the N.S.W. Government Printer, was lying
seriously ill at Guy’s Hospital.
The estate of “Ballarat Joe,” deceased,
was sworn at £538 personalty. It now transpires
that “Joe” was christened John —Cheswass.
George Ash, the journalist-M P. of S.A.
Parliament, whose name is known in every
squatter’s home in S. Australia, is seriously ill.
By the decision of the jury, the plaintiff
in the M’Meckan will-case (Melb.) gets some
£30,000 in lieu of the £lOOO left him by his
uncle.
Julian Thomas (The Yag.) is in very
indifferent health, and talks of lying up for a
week or two, as soon as his charities’ commission
work will allow him.
Countess Tolstoi recently besought the
Czar, on bended knee, to permit the free sale of
her husband's novels, and that iron-vested poten-
tate promised to see about it.
liiglis Clark, the clever Tasmanian
Attorney-General, “never drinks,” “never
smokes,” “ never gambles.” Then, in the name
of the Prophet, what docs he do?
Out of 36 members constituting the first
S. Australian House of Assembly, 13 still live.
Of the Legislative Council only three remain above
ground. Both Houses were constituted in 1857.
Sir Henry Parkes gushes over Edward
Smith Hall. Apropos, it would papear from certain
old MS. we have seen, that Edward Smith Hall
once sub-edited a Sydney daily paper—at 30s. per
week.
Colston’s defence was conducted entirely
without funds. To their honour be it said, the
medical experts all worked gratuitously. They
fought not to save a murderer, but to assert a great
principle.
The big family of sons born to Sir Alfred
Stephen have all been brought up to the family
profession—the law—excepting two, who found
congenial occupations in the Church and
commerce.
Sir Henry Parkes cuts up roughly when
reminded of his declining powers. Being told
the other day that he was a very much brighter
man in 1873 than in 1891, he complained of being
“liinsulted.”
Ten ex-governors are commemorated in
naming the new constituencies under the N.S.W.
Seats Redistribution Bill, also two judges, four
politicians, four explorers, and two old colonists
(Macleay and Redfern).
The death of Right Hon. John Inglis,
Lord President of the Scottish High Court of
Justice, is cabled. He was a great lawyer, and
in his day a great advocate. If we remember
rightly, he defended Madeline Smith.
The Nancy-like Dr. Cullen’s mincing
manner when ho is addressing the N.S.W.
Assembly makes everyone laugh. The laughter
is intensified when Harry Levien, the veteran
raconteur, interjects in fitting tone, “ Oh, you
naughty boy!” -
The portrait of Sir Henry Parkes in the
Sydney Exhibition is the historic work which
almost cost the country his life. It was while
he was riding to Mrs. Stoddard's studio on a
Sunday morning that his cab was upset and his
right leg broken.
It was lately cabled that Gladstone’s eldest
son “died under chloroform.” This was not so.
He succumbed after a desperate operation per-
formed on the brain with the view of removing a
tumour which had caused paralysis of the right
side for three years.
Fitzwilliam Wentworth has taken a
country house in the vicinity of Tunbridge Wells.
This does not look like returning to the land of
his fathers in a hurry. Sydney Burdekin is on the
look out for a big house, and he, too, contem-
plates a long stay in the old country.
Spurgeon, on his down-quilted spring bed
of sickness, has found comfort in reasserting his
belief in a red-hot material hell, and his conviction
that he’s booked for Heaven himself. A first-class
sample of fat, oily Christianity which gives brim-
stone to the many, and treacle to. the few.
Now that the jobbery of Sir John Mac-
donald & Go. in connection with the Canadian
Pacific Railway and otherwise is tolerably well
proved, it is interesting to learn that his post-
humous title of “Baron,” will descend to his
male heirs. Of such is the House of Peers.
E. B. Holt, late manager of the Bank of
New Zealand, Sydney, now occupies a financial
position to which a salary of £l2OO a year is
attached. The Bulletin is glad to hear of Holt
having thus partially recovered himself, as his
virtues an( i abilities far outbalanced his weakness,
A good story is told of Tasmanian Premier
Fysh. When new to office he received some
despatches from the Secretary of State for the
Colonies, signed Your most obedient humble
servant, Clarendon” —which were duly acknow-
ledged by “ Your most obedient humble servant,
Fysh.”

OUR NUISANCES.
MR. DEADBROKE (to his persecutor the hook-agent) : “ I don’t want it—have got one—
haven’t got time to look at it—besides, I couldn’t pay if I subscribed for
it—I am about to file my schedule. Don’t bother!”
BOOK FIEND : “Oh, you must subscribe, for old friendship’s sake. I’ll represent
your name as good. I want the commission; come now! ” —(Of course there
can be but one result.)
Mr. John Cape, who died at Launceston
recently at the age of 83, was a son of Mr. William
Cape, who was master of the Sydney Public
School in Castlereagh-strect in the early twenties.
In Lis young days he supplied the weather-records
to the Monitor! His brother, Mr. William T.
Cape, kept the well-known school in King-street
in which so many of the leading men of N.S.W.
were educated.
A lady tells The Bulletin that she
remembers going to private school with Madame
Melba in years gone* by. Melba had a decided
penchant for eating pork pies late at night, and
whistling in her sleep. (So we should think).
She also whistled during the day when the girls
marched two and two through the streets of East
Melbourne, and was many a time rebuked and
punished for said crime.
An old man well-known as “Tom’’and
proprietor of a barrow and boot box, who stood
for years at the corner of Park and Pitt Street,
Sydney, went to Maitland some time ago for
work. He lodged in a miserable hovel in the
Horseshoe Bend and one morning was found dead
in his bed. On examination he had secreted
about him no less than £5OOO, which, as he had
neither kith nor kin, went to swell Treasurer
M'Millan’s “surplus.”
Mr. Be Sails, the erudite squatter-legis-
lator, in N.S.W. nominee Council, bristling with
barristers “ I can understand why Christ, when
on earth, was not in favour of lawyers. His doc-
trine was evidently one that enforces equity upon
the world, and he reprimanded those people who
by quibbling perverted justice altogether.” Here-
upon the Hebraic Salomons (cheerfully): “I am
afraid the lion, member has access to papers and
books which I am not able to see.”
The Age London correspondent writes on
Adam Lindsay Gordon, as his most intimate
friend. Corrects Patchett Martin’s poetic, pic-
turesque account of how Gordon died. Patchett
writes like an eye-witness of how Gordon got up
at dawn, kissed his wife, took his gun and went
forth, with many other details—the whole thing
being imaginary, because he shot himself in the
evening, after being at the Yorick in the after-
noon. Gordon lost his money keeping a Ballarat
livery-stable.
On May 1, 1891, five persons said to be
over 100 years of age were inmates of the N.S.W.
Government Asylums. Two at Liverpool, one at
George-street, Parramatta, and two at Newmg-
ton. In the asylums on the same date were 48
persons between the ages of 90 and 100 years, four
only being females.
Sir Alfred Stephen, Lieut. -Governor and
ex-Chief Justice of N.S.W., who entered his
ninetieth year on 20th inst., arrived in Van-
demonia in 1824 (G 7 years ago !), and helped, as
Crown Solicitor, Solicitor-General, and Attorney-
General respectively of that hell-upon-earth, to
administer a bloody code in one of the most
bloody times of history. Yet, here is the old man
with all the traditions of the past weighing upon
his brain influencing and directing the criminal
legislation of N.S.W. in the year of grace, 1891.
The state of Spurgeon’s health as per
cable grows very wearisome. The other day a
prayer-meeting, lasting from 7 a.m. till 2 p.m.,
was held in London for his recovery. The Bul-
letin wishes the rev. gentleman well, but it can t
help saying that if he really has granular disease
of the kidneys, as stated, he might as well die at
once. It will save him ever so-much trouble. The
Almighty never cures granular disease of the
kidneys on any account, and the poor parson s
friends might as well pray for a slice of the moon.
Seven years ago, Patchett Martin’s article
rn Adam Lindsay Gordon introduced him, in
Temple Bar, to the English reading public. This
led to much Gordon material being showered on
Martin, who unloads in the latest Murray’s
Magazine. Gordon’s father sent him from home,
under 20, as a scapegrace, for illegally taking the
horse of one Black Tom, to ride in a steeplechase.
The poet’s wife was a poor girl, daughter of a Robe
(S.A.) working-man, and employed at a bush inn.
After Gordon’s death she married a Mr. Low,
who, so far as we can hear, is not a poet. Gordon
and she lost a daughter, aged two years, and there
were apparently.no other children.
They are going to have a close season for
kangaroo in S.A., while in the mother colony
they cannot be shot down fast enough.
Some of the old identities of Launceston
(Tas.) have just died off. In the last fortnight
there departed four persons over GO years of age,
nine over 70, eight over £O, and one over 00 !
The amalgamation of the Exchange Bank
of Australia, Ltd., with the Sydney Deposit Bank,
King-street, is a step in the right direction and as
following in the lino of the English banks, a great
number of whom are adopting this satisfactory
method of concentrating their energies and
capital. The total business of the Exchange
Bank has been merged into that of the Sydney
Deposit Bank, and under the able management of
Mr. T. S. Richardson, the General Manager, con-
tinued success is assured. The latest development
in banking has been promulgated by the Sydney
Deposit Bank who have for some time past given
practical effect to the principle of equitably
dividing banker’s profits with depositors’. The
Exchange Department is very interesting, and
travellers to all parts of the world are informed
that they can obtain or dispose of coins and notes
of all nations, also circular notes of exchange are
issued negotiable in all the principal cities through-
oK the world.*'
Within a radius of six miles from the
Adelaide Post-office there are 22 Wesleyan
churches.
The “genial” Willie Jinkins (everybody
in the theatrical business-manager line is genial
on principle) is just now calling the attention of
the nobility, gentry and public press —especially
p.p.—to a patent incandescent gas light, diffused
from tubes of platinum, or something, around the
vestibule of Melbourne Opera House. • The
advantages claimed for this new patent illuminat-
ing power are calculated to make any ordinary
gas consumer kill the rate collecting man next
time he comes round for the money. It is said to
cost no more than a simple nimble “bob” per
1000 feet, and, seeing that its steady mild beam
goes almost as far as the electric-light the patent
gas bids fair to enjoy a bright future, always
provided that the promoters are correct in their
calculations. The main ingredient being air,
there is no solid reason for questioning the present
estimate, because atmosphere is remarkably
plentiful and can always be laid on at a low price.
Prejudice is as varied in its operation as
ts victims are countless. Like sunshine and
rain, it falls upon the evil and the good ; but,
unlike the gifts of God, it is only fruitful of
mischievous • wrong. Many a sad and lonely
soul is wearing out to-day a life become pitiable
by prejudice. Many a bright hope and noble
aspiration has been blasted by its withering
influence. In the world around us there are
those who longed to do—aye, and would have
done—yeoman’s service for God and truth and
right had not the black shadow of prejudice
blocked their progress. Surely we may call it
a cruel and cowardly vice. Not only so, it is a
foolish one as well. Like justice—though in a
far different sense—prejudice is blind ; for,
while very potent to oppress and injure the
good, it just as often perhaps places the un-
worthy upon a pedestal of honour. Led by
the passing gossip of triflers, even well-inten-
tioned men, too lazy or too careless to examine
for themselves, surrender to its influence what-
ever fairmindedness they have, and thus private
reputations, social reforms, national affairs, and
even religious observances become the victims
of what is neither more nor less than criminal
thoughtlessness. Don’t be prejudiced against
Clements Tonic because it is a patent medicine,
or because some unscrupulous chemists and
doctors depreciate it. They only speak against
it because it pays them better to mix some of
their own concoctions ; and in this case you
have to take your chance of getting what may
suit your case, for it all depends whether they
may have the required drug in stock at the
time. Nqw r , this is not the case with Clements
Tonic, for every article required in the manu-
facture of this remedy is specially imported,
and used for no other purpose ; consequently
it is reliable and genuine. Clements Tonic does
not claim to cure everything ; but in many
special forms of disease it has been proved to
possess wonderful curative properties. There-
fore, is it not better for sufferers to use this
remedy, which has been proved reliable in
thousands of cases, than that they should be
experimented on by physicians and pseudo-
chemist-practitioners. You know you get a
genuine article in Clements Tonic, while it is
very doubtful if even the man who mixes your
medicine hap-hazard knows for certain what
he’s putting in the bottle Exceptions always
prove the rule, and an eminent physician writes
under date November 12, ’9O : “ Dear sir,—lt
gives me much pleasure to send you an acknow-
ledgment of the therapeutical value of Clements
Tonic. It behoves a medical man to be
extremely careful in lauding the value of any
patent medicine, the great majority being
entirely worthless. I have had, however,
practical demonstration in two cases of extreme
debility which have been under my personal
supervision, and which, I am happy to tell you,
made a wonderful rapid recovery by the use of
Clements Tonic. One very valuable end you
have achieved is that Clements Tonic can be
tolerated by an irritable stomach, which is so
often concomitant with atonic dyspepsia, and
which frequently debars the physician from
prescribing many valuable remedies, most of
which you have, however, combined in Clements
Tonic. In conclusion, permit me to congratu-
late you on the success you have obtained, and
which you thoroughly deserve. —I am, etc.,
Wiston Baker, M.D., C. M., L. R.C.S., L. R.C. P.,
Edin, (late surgeon A. A. Co., Newcastle),
Raymond Terrace, N.S.W. Mrs. E. Pike,
certificated midwife, Enmore (N.S.W.), writes ;
“ I have great pleasure in adding my testimony
to the efficacy of Clements Tonic. I suffered
from general debility and low fever, which had
afflicted me for two years, and Clements Tonic
set me up completely. lam never without it
in the house, and I find a few doses a capital
pick-me-up after fatigue, and I always recom-
mend it to my patients when it is needed.—
Yours truly, Mrs. E., Pike.” Clements Tonic
is sold everywhere. See the signature on labels
and wrapper.

Society
The fascinating divorced wife of a well-
known Sydney business man has “ chucked ” her
00-respondent, and now basks in the smiles of a
swell in the Guards. She is, writes a correspon-
dent, to be seen every Sunday very smartly dressed
at the Guards’ Club, Skindies, at Taplow, in com-
pany with her new Johnnie. When last seen poor
K. was gazing longingly and lovingly at her from
the opposite side of the Thames, at Maidenhead.
» # *
Item for a certain Melbourne man’s eye 1
A marriage has been arranged between Hon.
Frederick Rossmore Wauchope deMoleyns, of the
4th Hussars, A.D.C. to Lord Hopetoun (brother
of Lady Hopetoun) and Miss Millicent Wilson,
eldest daughter of Mr. Charles Wilson, of
Warter Priory, Pocklington, and M.P. for Hull.
Mr. Wilson is a member of the celebrated ship-
ping firm of Wilson Bros., of Hull, and brother
of the owner of Baccarat Hall, otherwise Tranby
Croft. The brothers Wilson are immensely
wealthy, and it is said that they consider it a
very bad year when they do not divide £400,000
between them. So Master de Moleyns, whose exit
from Melbourne took place under such auspicious
circumstances, has fallen right on his feet.
# # #
Our old friend, Benjamin Trovato, Esq.,
is now telling this story. The other day a
certain Australian dignitary—or shall we say
personage ?—whose wife somehow or other is not
received at local Government House, was talking
with His Ex., and the conversation drifted to the
subject of women drinking too much wine on
social occasions, particularly at Government
House. The personage deplored the custom,
“ and,” said he, “to mark my disapproval of it,
I do not allow Lady X. to visit Government
House.”

J. F. Patterson, the Melbourne artist,
thinks precious little of Sarah Bernhardt. At
that dismal Athenseum reception he craftily fixed
up a chair of honour right bang in front of one of his
masterpieces, and when Sara had gone away with-
out gushing over this noble work the little man
decided, once and for a’, that she no appreciated
airt, ye ken.

Apropos of something or other, impecuni-
ous Judges have sometimes found effective
methods of getting “even” with obdurate
creditors. A Victorian solicitor held the note of
a certain minor Judge for £5O. When due, a
renewal was refused and payment insisted upon.
The Judge waited his opportunity for revenge. 11
came. The creditor was sadly given to tippling,
and, when in his “cups,” was not overguarded in
his language. He had occasion to appear before
the minor Judge, for whom, by the way, he had
a most supreme contempt, and in the course of his
case made use of language which offended his
Honor’s ears. An apology was promptly de-
manded and as promptly refused, whereupon his
Honor sent the attorney “up ” for a fortnight,
and all the efforts of the friends of the latter
could not induce the Judge to relax the severity
of the sentence.
# # #
That story of the vagrant ex-policeman, in
Melbourne, reminds us of another member of the
force, a fine specimen of manhood in his prime,
a sergeant, who on all State occasions
attended the Governor and generally wore a
stand-off-the-grass air. He left the force
and took a public-house, the Scots Greys, in the
very worst part of Little Bourke-street. If he
thought the “push” would support him for his
own sake he was more or less mistaken. If they
did drink in his house they gave him —especially
the women—all sorts of trouble. The sergeant went
from bad to worse, was sold off, and died, poor
devil, in the most abject poverty and misery on
the bare floor of the Little Bourke-street drink-
shop !
* # #
A certain English peer, who not so very
Jong ago visited Australia, lost £87,000 in mining
deals with one Sydney broker alone. Among his
transactions were the purchase of 4000 Webbs, at
£4 10s. (they are now Is. or so); 3000 West
Wallsends, at 275. (now they’re Os.). He bought
Souths, at £l4 10s. (now they are £5). The mar-
vellous rise in Broken Hill Proprietary lured
him on.

# * #
The late Victorian Chief Justice, Sir
William Stawell, was a very mild man and seldom
gave way to surprise. He was, however, very
nearly knocked off his official perch in Chambers
one morning, when a well-known solicitor and his
clerk appeared before him to get his signature to
an order for a ca. re. (please note the difference
between Stawell’s act and that of the Judge in
Sydney who signed the order for ca. re. in the case
of the Actor-Managers, Gerald and Huff.) The
attorney had included 10 guineas in the document
as “ his costs ” when Stawell observed that he was
a Judge of the Supreme Court and not a taxing-
officer, and promptly ran his pen through the
amount. This was a little more than the attorney
bargained for, and forgetting the awful presence
in which hestood, said notto vocc, “B (hie) fool.”
Stawell stopped writing, and looking up said, in
his quiet way: “Hid I hear anyone speak?”
“Yes, your Honor,” promptly responded B——,
the clerk, “Mr. H——applied the remark to me
for my stupidity in inserting the costs.” Stawell
paused and presently said, “I accept the explana-
tion, but don’t let your principal appear before me
again in a state similar to that in which he now is,
or I may send him into retirement for a time.”
* * *
A Sydney law-clerk recently tried, day
after day, to serve a writ on a local builder, but
in vain. The defendant could not be found. At
last, encouraged by the promise of a new hat from
his employer, the scribe made another try, and
was successful, but it nearly cost him his life.
The builder was on top of a three-storey building
in course of erection, and the clerk, pretending
SHE KNEW IT.
PARSON : “It is not the body that needs most looking after, my dear young lady.”
SHE : “I know it isn’t—it’s the skirt.”
that he was the architect’s employe, climbed up
the shaky ladder. “ Are you Mr. B. ?” be queried.
“Yes,” said Mr. 8., with a smile. The pen-
wielder thereupon handed him the writ, displaying
at the same time the original. As he did so, he
saw a mischievous scowl darken B.’s face. He
understood it immediately and waited not a
moment. Down the ladder he went like a
monkey, with the defaulting builder close upon
him, threatening, in awful language, to “bust his
head in with a brick,” Once upon the ground the
clerk ran like a deer, Blank following him
with a big half-brick, and cursing like a
backblocker. It was a long run, but the clerk
got away alive, and won his hat. It was a tvvo-
and-sixpenny one, with a bashed crown, and thus
Capital got ahead of Labour once more, and the
toiler was again left.
# # *
Mr. W. H. Burgess, the chairman of the
unfortunate Y.D.L. Bank, is a big sufferer by the
failure of that old institution. He has not only
resigned his seat for West Hobart (with the re-
version of the Tasmanian Premiership in his
hands), but has even struck himself off the Com-
mission of the Peace. He has thrown up every
public position he held, discharged his servants,
removed his children from school, and put his
house in order for the grim finale. Mr. Burgess
has many sympathisers in the tight little isle.

that some of the ladies wore in ‘Evangeline’”—
or words to that effect.

ON THE BAY.
(For The Bulletin.)
Up with the jib, and slip the moorings.
Let her pay off till the canvass fills, so ;
Now she is feeling it, haul in that sheet there.
More, now belay ; and then, off we go.
Out on the water flashing with sunlight,
Out on the glistening rollers of green,
Down in the hollows, with wave-peaks around us,
Nothing but cloud-dappled blue to be seen ;
Up to the sky again, on to the crests of them,
Giddily reeling ; then the down-plunge again.
Everything drawing, ropes taut as muscle-cords,
Humming wind-music, so tight is the strain.
Eyes on the sail-hem, carefully feeling
Nearer and nearer the wind’s-eye she creeps,
While over the bows of her, into the skin of us,
Bubbling and soaking, the showering foam leaps.
All is the fun of it, all is the joy of it,
Oh ! for the rush through the salt driven spray.
The rolling ! the wetwind ! thesplashing ! the tossing !
There is no joy in life like a sail on the bay.
J. H. G.
*= * #
The partition of Sydney politically suggests
that Premier Parkes is perpetrating a jest at the
expense of the “ upper ten.” For voting purposes
lie has joined Woolloomooloo and Potts Point in
political wedlock, and caps his little joke by
calling the division after one of New South Wales’
most immoral Governors, whom he himself.once
denounced in this sturdy fashion: —“ It is
expedient that the Queen’s moral character —and
she is highly moral and virtuous by all report—
should not be represented by immorality
(Fitzroy). We believe it is the bounden duty of
all colonists who disapprove of the personal
characters of any officers of rank to abstain
from giving them countenance under pretext of
loyalty.”
* # #
Parkes was in a saturnine mood when he
selected the names for the new electoral divisions
of Sydney. Of the 11 divisions, only two beat
the names of pioneer politicians. “Slippery
Charley ” is perpetuated in the “Cooper” Divi-
sion ; and another division is named after good,
brave old Dr. Bland. Lang, Deniehy, Dailey,
Martin, Forster, Smith Hall, Lowe, Campbell,
are all forgotten, while narrow-minded Governors
like Gipps are honoured. The only excuse for
honouring Gipps’memory must be the similarity
of his disposition to Parkes’. For of Gipps it has
been written: —“Rude . , in his manners,
arbitrary in his disposition, and overbearing
in his demeanour. His moral sense also was
somewhat obtuse; and he was remarkably defi-
cient in the finer feelings and relentings of
humanity. His inferiors regarded him rather
with fear than with respect and reverence; and
while he succeeded in making many enemies, he
made no friends.” For the late Chief Justice
(then Mr. James) Martin’s opinion on Governor
Gipps. vide Barton’s “ Poets and Prose Writers of
N.S. Wales.”
* * *
The most beautiful and graceful ornaments
yet attached to any building, public or private,
in Australia are undoubtedly the half-draped
female figures which support the electric lamps
outside the Australia Hotel, Sydney, They form
indeed a lesson in art and an eloquent apology for
the absence of barmaids from the great hotel. A
curious legend attaches to these two beauties. It
is said that the management engaged in Europe
two Hebes of surpassing loveliness. Just ere they
were to depart for Sydney, “Ginger” 0 , a
well-known Australian gentleman, called at their
residence to say good-bye. On seeing “Ginger ”
the maidens at once turned into brass. Where-
upon they were packed in cases and sent along to
Manager Moore, who fixed ’em up outside his big
pub.
* # #
The fact that 20 men are now in Darling-
hurst prison for wife-desertion, and only two for
wife-and-child desertion, affords an interesting
glimpse of the main cause of marital unhappiness.
The Bulletin wonders what view is taken of this
aspect of the matrimonial question by the Woman
Suffrage Society ? From an independent woman
standpoint, how can it be reasonably argued that
any man should be put in gaol for deserting his
wife if they have no children ?

Another parcel of amateur white mis-
sionaries (chiefly women, of .course) are about to
leave the sin-stained city of Sydney with all its
miserable poverty-stricken wives and children for
China, in the charge of a clergyman. Six more
missionary women follow in September, under the
charge of another parson. How affectingly
beautiful! And it’s all in the interests of that
bumptious, organised hypocrisy, the London
Missionary Society, advance-agents for the same
pious J. Bull who not so long since forced opium
on China at the point of the bayonet.
* # #
Maria Island (Tas.) graveyard—weird,
wild, silent and sad —faces the great ocean looking
towards Maoriland. Here may be seen six old
blackened grave-stones, bearing the names of six
Maori chiefs, transported there for trying to drive
out the invaders of their fatherland in 1846. An
old inhabitant of the island says that the saddest
sight that ever met mortal eyes was to see these
brawny, dignified men sitting on the high, rocky
cliffs where now they sleep—longingly and sullenly
gazing towards their lost homeland.

name !

It has been specially remarked of late
that, thanks to the forethought of a former
Governor, two of the private spare-bedrooms at

* # #
One of the successful bowlers for Harrow
in the recent annual cricket match, writes our
English correspondent, was young Rome, son of
“Tom ” Rome, formerly of Northampton Downs,
Queensland. He is the youngest lad who has
ever taken part in the schools match, being only
a little over 15 years of age. 'He is six feet high,
and when he has thickened out a bit will develope
into a bowler of the first class. He justified his
selection by taking five wickets for a good average.
His father was until last year the popular master
of the Cotswold Hunt (“ I remember the lowering
wintry morn and the mist on the Cotswold hills,”
sang A. L. Gordon), and a better man across
country cannot be found, Pat Osborne used to
hunt with this pack, but it can hardly be said that
he kept up the reputation of Australia for horse-
manship, though he rode the very best cattle.
* # #
The Trinity Hall (Cambridge) crew which
won the Visitors’ Cup at Henley the other day,
was composed of three thorough-bred Australians,
Garrick (son of Sir James), Ned Lord, and Lan-
dale, whilst the fourth man, Fog-Elliott, who
stroked the crew, is the son of a lady who was
born in Australia. The cooees which greeted their
victory might have been heard a mile off. Aus-
tralia was well represented afloat and ashore.
Mrs. Bassett (a sister of W. C. Wentworth) has a
house just at the winning-post. The Letts—
Charles A. W. L. is still beautiful as ever—have
a cottage close by ; Sir James Garrick shared a
house-boat with Agent - General Samuel, and
Mount Morganaire Knox D’Arcy had a “swag-
ger ” one all to himself. The Fred. Want’s were
on board the Albany Club house-boat, and dis-
pensed hospitality freely. There were hosts of
other “colonials” present. The difference between
a colonial and an Australian will be explained
when we have more time.
* * #
Melbourne papers carefully refrained from
reporting a spicy little case in which a certain
newspaper man figured as defendant. A young
female, describing herself as an ex-virgin, charged
Jimmy with being the author of her shame, but
the allegation broke down in all directions, the
aged father’s curse was dismissed as a put-up job,
the tears of the fond mother for her “ruined”
child fell upon stony ground, and James left the
court with a character still unspotted before the
world. So completely, in fact, was his innocence
established that James’ successful endeavours to
keep the matter out of print can only be attributed
to his abnormal modesty. Few evenin’ newspaper
men would, under similar circumstances, have
shown such self-denial. Why, in the name of all
that’s good and pure, didn’t the Howl burst
forth with a speshul edishun, containing a true
and particular account of this interesting episode,
headed “Virtue Again Triumphant!” and illus-
trated with portraits of the Snow-White Male and
the Black Fe-Mailer ?

m. *
The divorce-case of Crawley v. Crawley,
just finished in Dunedin (M.L.), is a solemn
subject and full of pathos. The parties were
married in 1886, and the bride wept all through
the ceremony. She didn’t want to be married,
but having promised in a moment of aberration,
she was too young and too much frightened to
back out. When the ceremony was over she was
still weeping. She howled bitterly on the door-
step outside the church, and attracted a crowd.
Her husband got excited, and hustled her away
to the nearest quiet spot, which happened to be
the cemetery. It was a damp, sodden, winter
day, and the trees were bare and shrivelled, and
the earth was slushy, and there was ice on the
tombstones, and the inscriptions were frozen hard.
It was one o’clock when the happy pair reached
this place, and the bride wailed among the sepul-
chres until six. Then Crawley threw up his con-
tract and left. His new-made wife was flabby
with weeping, and as limp as a strip of blotting-
paper, and her lugubrious howls in the solemn,
windy twilight, among the homes of the dead
inhabitants, were too much for Crawley. He laid
clown his hand. He quitted, in short. He left
bis bride, and his situation, and his house and
furniture and everything else, and vanished.
Nobody saw him or heard of him again. And
now the law has granted a divorce on the ground
of desertion, which is an unkind way of putting
it, seeing that the real causes were damp and
incipient rheumatism.

A certain mental specialist, of Melbourne,
said, on first seeing murderer Colston : “ If he had
come into my study casually, I would have at
once signed his certificate of insanity.” Yet he
declined to give evidence at the trial, and the
defence had no funds to make his evidence suffi-
ciently spontaneous to call him !
# « #
Young Lord Hope rode on one of the
young St. Bernard dogs during the late Mel-
bourne show. Ten minutes after the dog’s ticket
was marked “Sold—price £40.” It was worth
about a tenner—outside price. Happy is the man
who has a lord to ride his pups.
# * #
The Victorian authorities decline a post-
mortem of Colston. They declined it in murderer
Johnston’s case, as he objected. Colston wishes
it, and they still decline. This is not the action
of a civilised Government.
# # *
With much reluctance we print the
following :
Dear Bulletin, — Despite all that has been said to the
contrary I firmly believe that every woman, whether
happily married or otherwise, has secret hankerings after
divorce. Women are so sentimental, so romantic, that
even after bearing dozens of children, losing hair and
teeth, and running into 20 stone or so of too solid flesh,
they imagine that some handsome bold-eyed boy is likely
to fall in love with them could they only obtain a suffi-
ciently bewitching costume. Of course, they wouldn’t
do anything wrong, oh, dear no ; therefore they would
like to be able to obtain dissolution of the marriage con-
tract on the grounds of incompatibility of temper or
snoring in slumber, or some otherwise grave cause of
domestic disagreement. Meantime, they consult “the
cards,” and console themselves with fortune-tellers. —
Yours, A Much-Married Man.

# • •
A melancholy death occurred at the Alfred
Hospital, Melbourne, not long ago. The doctor
forbade fruit to a typhoid patient, but the latter’s
friends brought in grapes, and joked him into
eating one or two, when bowel-perforation and
death ensued.
# * *
There was one mysterious female in the
famous baccarat party at Tranby-Croft who,
apparently, will never be cleared up. None of
the witnesses ' mentioned her name—she was
merely alluded to as “a player.” None of the
lawyers demanded her name in cross-examination,
though why they didn’t no one can explain. She
was officially smothered and put out of sight, and
whether she was too shady to be mentioned, or
too important to be exposed, or too particular a
friend of the Prince of Wales to be spoken of, can
only be conjectured. One theory is that she was a
princess out on the “bust” at midnight in an
atmosphere of liquor and long, black cigars ; the
other theory is that she was an actress with a
damaged reputation. Anybody who could clear
up this point would probably be made a colonial
Governor to keen him quiet.

The stoppage of the Sydney Hospital
buildings in 1833 has been a costly experiment for
N.S.W., for the interest alone on the capital
invested in the unfinished and unavailable works
has totalled £20,990! The buildings, in their in-
complete state, represent £69,218 of borrowed
money.
# * *
Refreshment catering on the Victorian
railways is to be amended after December 31,
when the weary traveller, instead of squirming
over a ghastly caricature of a chop, or a few
greasy slabs of dusky beef at eighteenpence, will
get a clean, discretionary meal for two-and-six,
the N.S.W. tariff.

* * *
Hobart Polo Club in full blast is a sad
spectacle. Very few of the men can ride, and
nearly all the nags served their time in cabs or
bakers’ carts. They recall the mokes with which
the field-artillery, sent by Australia to the Soudan,
was horsed, and which would never gee-up until
the driver made a noise like a door-bang.

son-in-law.

A Melbourne paper suggests the substitu-
tion of ginger-wine, lemonade, and other tem-
perance drinks, for champagne at balls, etc. In
view of what has occurred on several occasions
lately at Sydney “ chivoos,” in consequence of
the plenitude of “fizz,” a re-action in favour of
“soft” drinks, for ladies especially, has ret in
amongst hostesses (headed by Lady Jersey)
of the City of the Beautiful Harbour. Can
it be the aptitude of very high - toned
young ladies down South to indulge in
step-dancing in the streets after the formal close
of social festivities, which prompts a change in
the style of refreshments?
# # *
The new swell dance, performed to the
air of “the Bogie Man,” is the Angels’ and Demons’
Waltz. The angels arc attired in loose, flowing
robes of white, with silver girdles and stars in
their hair, flowing wing sleeves, and a big spray
of lilies in their hands. The devils are got-up
with long black forked tails, while tufts of hair
on either side of the head give the idea of pointed
ears. Black coats, with a kind of bat’s wing
under the arm and joined to the side, black bands
of silk across the shirt front, to cover all gleams
of white knee breeches, silk stockings and
pumps.

The “ royal christening ’ of the Duchess
of Fife’s firstborn was a delirious function, appar
ently. When the infant (who is exceedingly
doughy and unwholesome) was being whirled
away from the tabernacle in a closed carriage the
Archbishop of Canterbury took part in a general
waving of handkerchiefs. The Queen is said to
have smiled graciously at his holy enthusiasm, and
the Almighty also made a note of it, no doubt.

The Bulletin begs to acknowledge, with
a prolonged shriek, the receipt of the Richmond
and Twickenham Times Supplement, dated J une
13th, 1891. This damp sheet (for it is wet with
cur bitter tears) was issued in celebration of the
silver wedding of the Duke and Duchess of Teck,
and contains beautiful portraits of that revered
couple and family, both individual and collective ;
also, portraits of the couple before they had a
family, and sundry reproductions of old pictures
illustrating incidents of their marriage day. Many
columns of printed matter, commencing—“ Now
that aU England is rejoicing with the Duke and
Duchess of Teck in the celebration of their silver
wedding,” assist to make the aforesaid supple-
ment an historical souvenir and a joy for ever.
Wherever the English language is spoken this
illustrated narrative deserves to find purchasers.
It is essentially touching. The fact that a bank-
rupt German Serenity should hang on to the same
old wife for a quarter of a century must send a
thrill of satisfaction through every taxpayer who
c ontributes his mite towards the support of their
c ome stic bliss. Australia, as well as the beloved
mother country, glories in the blessed thought
that Teck was happily restored to the arms of the
Queen’s cousin after he had had a row with her
over an actress some fifteen years ago. But for
Tummy’s thoughtful behaviour in bringing Teck
back to the Royal Family circle on that occasion
we should have had no national rejoicings, no
provincial newspaper supplement, no reminder
that a fat, unintellectual pair of sold-up loafers
continued to exist to point a democratic moral and
adorn a darned penny-royal tale.
# # *
Adelaide Mayor’s Ball, oil August 19, was
the finest social spectacle yet seen in the sacred
city. Mayor Cohen spent money lixe water, the
little bill probably amounting to £3500. There
were over 2000 guests.
# * #
A low-grade N.B.W. constable, dismissed,
after six years’ faithful service, from a Chinese-
haunted beat, for drunkenness, has since drawn
£670 from the bank, his honest, hard-earned
savings.
A LETTER.
Melbourne, August 22, 1891.
Dear Heditor, —I’m in a scot, O I’m sweatin’ an
dasliin’ my gaiters,
For a man down at Brunswick has got nearly
rooined for sellin’ pertators
On Sunday, a week or two back, to some little
hinnercent sinner,
Whose mother was cookin’ a snack, and wanted
two pen’orth for dinner.
The h’infant goes todderlin’round with a bag (as
the riter supposes)
And the shopkeeper weighs out two pound, for-
gettin’ the Bible an’ Moses,
But Nemmersis comes in the guys of a h active
intellergent ‘-copper,” „ , ,
He twigs the transackshun an sighs, My Bora ’
this is ’ighly improper.”
So he took out a summuns, he did, and carried it
round to the dealer
Who’d traded off spuds to that kid, without givm’
a thought to the “ peeler,”
And, cuttin’ a long story short, this here was the
magerstates’ sentence —
A fiver paid down into Court, or a month in the
cells for repentance.
Tee kulprit looked calm and resigned, he ’adn’t
the ’art for resistance,
He borrered the “ quids ” he was fined, an’ faded
away in the distance ;
And, as for the worshipful beaks, I’m told (by a
pal as reported)
They blew out their worshipful cheeks an’ looked
up to ’Eaven and shorted.
I've stated the facks o’ the case, and now you can
underconstumble
Why I cuss till I’m black in the face—it’s enuff
to make any man grumble ;
If this country we live in is free, as they tells us
in speeches and sermuns,
Why, I wish I was over the sea, along with the
French and the Germuns.
It’s O ! to be down-trodden slaves in lands where
there’s no Missis Grundy,
Where a cove can get liquors, an’ shaves, an’
“smokes,” an’ pervishuns on Sunday,
And order a smokin’ ’ot roll, or buy his young
woman a present;
' Out here they look after your soul in a way wot is
’orrid unpleasant.
Dear Heditor, I shall “ levant ” (that’s furrin for
“ slope ”) from a region
Of tyranny, ’umbug, an’ cant, an’ bye laws for
spreadin’ religion ;
Such laws may be ’onestly meant, but they ain’t,
I don’t think, the Creator’s,
For He wouldn’t fine any gent, for a-sel!in’ two
pounds o’ pertators.
So if I don’t write any more you'll know by this
’ere little token
That I’m orf to a wickeder shore where the dear
“ mother tung ” isn’t spoken :
An’ if I ’ang on where I am you had best keep a
h’eye on my verses,
Tor I’m bound to be droppin’ in “dam,” an’
ventin’ mv h’anarer in curses.
E. F.
“Radam’s Microbe Killer.”
22nd January, 18!)].
00 and 02 Elizabeth-street,
Melbourne.
Rad am "s Microbe Killer Co.
Gentlemen,—Home time since you kindly gave
to an applicant of the Melbourne Jewish Philan-
thropic Society two jars of your “ Microbe
Killer.” The unfortunate man was in a bad state
from consumption, and very little hopes were
entertained for his ultimate recovery. After
taking five jars of your “Microbe Killer ” he found
great relief, and, continuing with same, is now all
but thoroughly recovered and able to earn a living
for his wife and family. You are at perfect liberty
to make what use you think tit of this testimonial,
and I can vouch for its correctness in every
particular.—Yours, &c.,
S. DAVIES
(of Lazarus, Rosenfeld, and Benjamin),
Treas. Melbourne Jewish Philanthropic Society.
Pamphlets sent free. Head office ; 40 Elizabeth-
street, Melbourne, .agencies ; 319 George-street,
Sydney ; 17 Hunter-street, Newcastle ; 6!) Queen-
street, Brisbane, etc.


STUDY AND PERSEVERANCE
REWARDED.
Herr Rassmussen, the celebrated Danish herba-
list, is undoubtedly one of the most hardworking
and deserving among Australian scientists. His
Danish herbal remedies are too well and favour-
ably known to the public to require any addi-
tional praise. His herbal discoveries which
are numerous, have already given many hands
employment from tune to time, one of which is to
extract a purer eucalyptus oil from our gum-trees
than any which has hitherto been produced This
improvement is brought about with his newly-
invented eucalyptus extractors and condense/s.
lo manufacture this improved eucalyptus oil
Herr Rassmussen is about to open large
eucalyptus works at his estate at Minto, where
experiments are now being carried on very
successfully. _ Tasmanian blue gum trees
have been imported and planted there for
the last five years, and they are thriving
splendidly ISo doubt this extensive under-
' Vl bo , ns s '! ccessfnl as a 'l his previous
undertakings have been, and his new product
will find its way throughout the world, as his
celebrated Denmark herbal remedies have done
Herr Rassmussen has just opened a very large
establishment at 047 George-street, Sydney, for the
sale of his well-known Denmark herbal remedies
and it us not only the most scientifically-complete
establishment of its kind in Sydney, but it
is also the most pretty and artistic to be seen in
Australia. Large crowds of eager onlookers are
seen inspecting the interesting windows contain-
ing his wonderful microscope, his numerous
Government registrations for his remedies, and
his hundreds of testimonials, as well as the mag-
nificent gold medal awarded to him. Sufficient
proof will be seen m his windows of the entire
genuineness and reliability of his remedies, which
are now patronised by the whole world. The
magnificent fittings of this establishment were
carried out faithfully and well by Mr. Peter
homle cf this city. Herr Rassmussen may well
be proud, of his new establishment.

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
“ Pendragon,” of the Referee, who visited us
i few years ago, and is now dead, cut Up rich (saj s our
correspondent), the personalty amounting to nearly
£5O 000; realty not stated, though it must amount to a
considerable sum. He leaves his wife a life interest in his
estate, with remainder to his adopted daughter, who has
■lower of appointment. In the event of her death, with-
out having exercised such power, the estate is to be
divided into three parts, of which G. 11. Sims and Dick
Butler, who has been for years “Pen.’s” righthand man
and friend, take one-third each, and the remaining third
coes to two or three charities connected with the printing
t nde Considering that the Referee has been in exist-
ence only about 15 or 18 years, and that for a great por-
tion of that time Sampson had only a comparatively small
interest in the paper, some idea may be formed of what a
p-rand property it is. Already one misses the master-
hand. In the natural order of things men write them-
selves out, and even Sims’ liver begins to cease to have
anv interest for people who buy a paper for the sake of
the news it contains. Martin Cobbett still contributes
the “ Sporting Notions,” and the “ Handbook” continues
to be a leading feature of the front page. Probably
Sims will not write for the paper much longer. With
all his love of money, he begins to find the work
irksome. Besides, he is “beastly rich,” and can afford
to forfeit his stipend from the Referee. Martin Cobbett,
besides writing for several papers, including the Mel-
bourne Age, is a turf-commissioner, and makes a large
income as such. He does business for some big men and
is generally in the know. Occasionally he is put in the
cart and you can always tell when this is the case by his
assumption of high tone and purity when he writes in the
“ Darebin ” Guesdon is back in Hobart again,
where he has many friends and one or two deadly foes.
* * *
Larry T'oley is no end of a speculator, and if
anyone goes to him with a “ good thing,” Laurence, as a
rule, has the pluck to grip it. However, a gentleman
recently returned from the Islands came to Larry, the
other day, and unfolded a scheme for annexing real
estate that even the ex-champion had not the pluck to
tackle. During his trip to the Islands the traveller had
come across an island, “ a regular little daisy of a place,”
to quote his own words, “ with only about 40 inhabitants,
ruled by a decrepid old chief.” The plan of annexation,
as unfolded to Larry in all seriousness, was that the two
of them should start from the neighbouring islands in a
cutter laden with a cask of poisoned rum, and that such
of the inhabitants who escaped the rum should he done
to death with the shooting irons, when the island could be
jumped. Larry admitted the millions that were in it, but
he nevertheless passed.
* * *
At Sydney Sailing Club’s meeting the other
day “there was a discussion as to the regulations recently
gazetted by N.S.W. Marine Board for the regulation of
hired sailing boats, those present expressing considerable
indignation at the class legislation shown. It is a well-
known fact that hired sailing boats are the best built and
best found boats of their class in the harbour, and when
these boats are out of date, they generally find their way
into the ranks of private boat owners. Why these boats
should be selected by the Marine Board to legislate on,
and private boats with defective sails and gear be allowed
to do what they like, is at present a mystery.” It is no
mystery. “ Hired” boats are often let to a crowd of in-
competents, who carry a lot of helpless women as pas-
sengers-each man or boy having his girl with him.
Private boats are usually sailed by experts (men who own
boats soon learn to sail them), and as they, as a rule,
carry fewer persons than hired boats, there is less danger.
# * *
Dan Cooper told cricketer Barrett that he con-
sidered the quality of the English racehorse, all round, to
be far superior to that of the Australian article, although
a wonder like Carbine would doubtless hold his own in
the best of company.” And even Carbine is as English as
they can make ’em in New Zealand, a fact which The
Bulletin is not too nationalistic to remember.
# * #
The sporting world relapsed into a cynical
smile when Mr. Willie Jones recently announced that
Dreadnought had partially broken-down. Check-suited
philosophers called to mind how Bravo nearly broke
down a week before winning the Melbourne Cup, and
some of them rushed off to back Dreadnought on the
strength of his infirmities. Now that the Australian Cup
hero.of 1889 has indeed “gone for ever” the word of
Jones is held sacred in tobacconist’s shops, and the
double-event layers sing praises to his name.

At the Sydney Bicycle Sports Kerr, who won
the 8.8. C. mile championship and the mile championship
cf|N.S. Wales in 2min. 52 l-ssec. and 2min. 53 l-ssec.,
afterwards, in a race with time, put up the Australian
record for a grass mile—2min. 48 2-ssec. In the mile
footrace.!. M. Andrews won with 145yds. start, and the
second man, J. F. Dalrymple, made the Australian amateur
record—4min. 25sec.
# # #
A certain wealthy sporting man gave, for many
years, ten guineas to buy a trophy at a certain annual
metropolitan dog-show. ' This year his name does not
appear. The story goes that a man, last year, walked
into the sporting man’s office, and, hanging down a plated
mug, asked him what he meant by giving that as a ten-
guinea tronhy. Mr. B. was thunder-stricken, and ex-
plained that he gave his cheque for ten guineas. The
mug was second-hand—value, say, 15s. Someone “got
the run,” and Mr. B. and others who heard the story
withdrew from the show.
# # *
The first stag-hunt ever attempted in Maoriland
came off the other day near Wellington, with much eclat.
The local Hunt Club fetched along the stag, and turned
it out in front of a great assemblage of dogs, idiots, and
money-lenders. The animal, of course, should have
bolted, and then all the dogs and Hebrews would have
galloped and howled like blazes in the rear; but instead
it started to eat grass by the roadside. Somebody
walloped it with a whip, and it ran into a paddock and ate
some more grass. Then the local small boys and cads and
larrikins tried to scare it away with screams and brickbats
and bottles, and at last it was induced to go off at a
canter. The dogs cantered also, and the uncles and
clerks and tailors swore and thrashed and gal-
loped and perspired in the rear. Next, the tame
stag hid itself among some cows, but being
yelled at and dog-bitten it went off again, and
after cutting itself on' various wire-fences it was overtaken
by the dogs and bitten some more. Then the show ended,
but the wretched animal died that night from fright and
misery, and being chawed at by mongrels, and yelled into
a premature grave by pawnbrokers. The one redeeming
feature about the show is that Governor Onslow wrote to
the club, and gave it most particular sheol, and Tub
Bulletin - trusts this fact will be remembered in his favour
and writ large on his tombstone. For it is better to give
a lift to a harassed brute that is being welted with fence-
rails by the upper ten in the name of sport than it is to be
a K.C M.G. and sing the “ National Anthepi ” till one
bursts.
J. 0. Fergusson, S.M., formerly deputy-
collector of Customs, and latterly clerk of Adelaide Police
Court, who died last week, was in his time one of the best
rifle-shots in Australia. He had been in Government
employ for 42 years, had been twice retrenched, and at
his death was receiving the munificent salary of £280 per
annum.
# # *
A yell of execration arises at the Adelaide
police authorises’ action with regard to the bookmaking
fraternity. No betting is now allowed outside the grand-
stand, with the result that the wagering is almost
entirely restricted to the half-guinea division ; the public
—who are the main supporters of the bookmakers by cash
betting—being almost wholly debarred from putting their
money on. The result is that the races are poorly
attended, and excitement-greatly on the wane. They
always did make a hash of racing in S. Australia.

John L. Sullivan’s experiences prove that Aus-
tralians may appreciate a good boxer without getting
enthusiastic over a bad actor.
