for Slavin or Mitchell. He has put up a £200 forfeit.
K EEP IT FOR REFERENCE
The following suggestions are not designed to
induce the public to attempt the duties of the
regular surgeon, but merely to place the reader in
possession of a means of treatment of the minor
accidents occurring daily in the household. No
family can be assured against accidents; the
broken bone, the dislocated joint, the severed
artery—these are misfortunes that may possibly
happen to anyone; and we scarcely need to add
that they demand thp instant attention of the
most skilful surgeon within roach. But by house-
hold accidents we mean those which are constantly
occurring every day, in nearly every family, and
which, while not always dangerous in themselves,
are exceedingly annoying. Burns, bruises, cuts,
scalds, sprains, &c., are principal among these
troublesome and annoying occurrences, and
demand immediate treatment with the best means
at hand. In the kitchen, the dining-hall, th e
nursery, and the sitting-room they are liable to
happen, and instead of fear and alarm at the sight
of the cut or smashed finger, or burned or bruised
arm, or scalded surface, a cool and quiet manner
should be assumed, and after washing away the
blood (if required), the injured parts should be
dressed with that most valuable remedy—St.
Jacobs Oil. Its especial worth for the injuries
named, and others of similar nature, is not based
upon mere claim and assertion ; but its constant
use for a long term of years has given it a reputa-
tion which is always increasing. Its surprisingly
quick relief, its cleansing properties, its tendency
to quickly remove all inflammation, and its
wonderful efficacy in the above, as well as in all
muscular and other pains, such as rheumatism,
neuralgia, toothache, headache, stiffness of the
limbs, joints, &c., these qualities render St.
Jacobs Oil pre-eminently the best external remedy
before the people, which claim is fully sub-
stantiated by the class of strong testimony
voluntarily accorded it by representatives of every
class and station in life.
THE PANGS OF CONSCIENCE.
There is no necessity to commit wilful murder,
or to speak in a loud and angry tone of vo ce to
one’s mother-in-law, to understand how Richard
111. felt on the bloody field of Bosworth, when
admitting that
“ My conscience hath a thousand several tongues,
And every tongue brings in a several tale,
And every tale condemns me for a villain.”
Any man, whether he be a king or a contractor
for the supply of shoddy stores to a trusting
Government, must feel pretty bad before he can
talk like that, although Richard was only talking
to himself after a bad night passed in company
with an undigested supper and a queer I t of
ghosts begotten thereof. And it is really astonish-
ing what a number of people do accuse themselves
in this drivelling fashion. Some do it out loud,
but unconsciously. Take themselves into their
own confidence as it were, while walking along
the streets in pursuit of their ordinary avocations,
or while sitting at the table at the mercy of the
smart waitress, who has been away for ten or
fifteen minutes in chase of another cup of tea or
some other painfully common-place hash-house
commodity. It is at such times as these that the
pangs of conscience are apt to break loose in the
system, and run a wild riot among the heart's
best feelings ; for no matter how much the best
man may struggle to hide himself from his friends,
there are moments when self-control becomes as
impossible to the conscience-stricken man as to
the one whose epigastrium has gone back on him
in a heavy head sea on board a small coasting
steamer. Conscience makes cowards of some, and
only fools of others. It makes the worst kind of
coward of the man who is mean and reckless
enough to curtail his household expenses to the
extent of lighting up his home and family with
bad kerosene, ami who in consequence thereof
arrives at the garden ga.te some night just in time
to see all his household belongings careering
madly skywards through a rent in the roof as a
first result of the cat having knocked the lamp
down. His conscience will never let him forget
that, and serve him right too ! He can avoid it
if he will by burning only Luxcne, the new
illuminant, which is beautiful to the eye, pleasant
to the nostrils, economical to the pocket, and safe
in every direction. Corroborative testimony on
application to Messrs. J. Barlow and Co., Tillock
and Co., T. W. Eady and Son, Mason Bros.,
Ltd., I). Mitchell and Co., J. Keep and Sons, J.
G. Hanks and Co., Holdsworth, MTherson and
Co., M'Lean Bros, and Rigs?, Ltd., Fred. Butcher,
and Robert Miller, Sydney ; and J. Ireland, R.
Hall and Son, and David Miller, Newcastle.

The great Maoriland sea-serpent has “a
black back, white belly, two long arms and a flat
head. A missing politician.
According to the late Peter Lalor, the
nationalities of the killed and wounded at the
Eureka Stockade were as follows : Irishmen, killed
10, wounded 10; Englishmen, killed 1, wounded 2 ;
Scotchmen, killed 1, wounded 0; Prussians,
killed 2, wounded 0; N. S. Wales, killed 1,
wounded 0; Canadians, killed 2, wounded 0.
Iwo men were killed whose nationalities were un-
known and three killed whose names and country
were unknown.
Significant proposition tabled in N.Z.
Parliament:—
That, in order to give an impetus to and permanently
an^'cll^ure > all N.Z. products (and people?)
should he carried free upon the railways of the colony,
and that a commercial marine-service be organised to
connect with the railways; also that the revenue
required to provide interest and maintenance, short of
that derived from other sources in this service, be made
up from a special land-tax.
Ihe argument is that there is no more reason for
charging the people for the use of their own
railways than there is for charging them for the
use of the public roads. Moreover, a system of
free conveyance by rail would tend to promote
settlement in the country, and check the present
fatal tendency toward concentration of the popu-
lation in large towns and cities.
In one of the colonies of which he was
Governor in his youth, Sir George Grey and a party
came to a river which was difficult to ford, and at a
spot where it never had been forded. Right there
the Governor wanted to ford it, and, as he always
had his own way, his companions left him, went
to the regular crossing place further up, and
reached a shanty on the other side. An houi
or two passed and the Governor didn’t tun
up. Evidently something had happened tc
him. The party went out to look for his
corpse, and had been away some little time
when a horseman, who had manifestly been
having a bad wet time in deep water, rode
up to the shanty and, as he dismounted, casually
asked the proprietor whether there was any news.
“Oh> yes, sir; there’s great news!” said the
man. “ ihey think the Governor’s drowned in
the river. We shall get a bridge now.” “And
that’s what you call good news, is it?” said his
visitor, good-humoredly. “ Well, I don’t think it
is. lam the Governor.”
Dear Bulletin,—You have ere now noted the
fjreat anxiety of Chief Justice Darley to purify the atmo-
sphere of the N.S.W. Supreme Court and its neighbour-
hood- Inside the court rooms his Honor lias been very-
thorough in his renovation. The old, time-worn seats to
which clung memories of the Martins, Butlers, Daileys
Broadhursts, and a host of other brilliant barristers, have
given place to highly-varnished, newly-smelling “pews,”
on which are labelled the respective classes of persons
permitted to occupy them. They are, however, seldom
occupied now. The legal learning of Martin, the wit of
Dailey, the rugged eloquence of Butler, and the rounded
periods of Darvall are wanting to attract audiences at the
Supreme Court, even if the C.J. had left room for the
decent portion of the public. A stranger, however respec-
table, on entering the court-room, is pounced upon by
one of the sheriff's myrmidons (in many cases foully reeking
of tobacco and stale beer), a id if the visitor be not a limb of
the law, a J.P. ora pressman, he is incontinently told
there is no room, though nearly all the seats are empty •
hut he may find a porch up in the c ck-loft, dignified by
the name of gallery. There is, however, one more reform
which the Chief Justice might undertake with advantage
to people having business at court, and with persons who
have to use King-street on business or pleasure. Every
day, whether the courts be sitting or not, the verandah
of the court-building exhibits a crowd of unwashed un-
kempt men whose eyes “ purge thick amber and plum-
tree gum.” They even invade the path in front ot
the building, and not infrequently- find their ways
to the opposite side of the street, blocking up the road
and generally inconveniencing the public. On inquiry we
are told that these gentry are bailiffs of the Supreme
Court, or rather the “ men ” that the S.C. bailiffs put in
“possession,” when some unfortunate gets within the
clutches of the civil law. In Melbourne, I understand',
the Sheriff provides a room where these necessary if
otherwise harmless, individuals can seat themselves till
wan ted. They are not allowed to hang about the doors
of Melbourne’s palace of justice, and I think that Chief-
Justice Darley or the Sheriff might, with profit, intro-
duce both the room and the rule in the Sydney Supreme
Court.—Yours, Btsn Lawyer.

Civilization is still on the march. At
newly-established Camooweal (N.W.Q.), at latest
dates the police had seven Chows and two white
men “chained up to verandah-posts like dogs.”
The white-men’s mates were pouring “snake-
juice” down their throats and the Chows were
playing euchre.
Pentecost satirises the dogmatists
Bok is. Bok created man a living slork. After man
dies he will pass into the land of harmulkins and dwell
with the juflams forever. Can anybody understand
that? Certainly not; became nobody knows what cer-
tain of the words mean. Until those words can be
defined, the sentences in which they appear cannot mean
anything. I made up some sentences like the foregoing
a few days ago, and asked a man if he understood what
they meant. He said he did. I asked him to explain
them to me. He said he could not do that, but that if 1
would open my harraulkinal eyes I would understand
them. I asked him what my harraulkinal eyes were, and
he told me that could only be harmulkinally discerned.
And I could see that he was very earnest in what he was
saying, and that he thought he knew what he was talking
about—hut I didn’t.
Secured Deposits. —ln view of the promised
legislation to regulate the business of those finan-
cial institutions which receive money on deposit
from the public, we are pleased to notice that a
step in the right direction has been taken by the
Universal Land and Deposit Bank, Limited, who,
in a little pamphlet entitled “Mutual Banldng,”
have called attention to a few points of practical
interest to all bank depositors. The main idea of
the above company appears to be that the
depositors, not the shareholders, should be the
first to receive the benefit of the profits made by
the bank, whilst, by the issue of debentures, they
(the depositors) obtain a first lien over all the
assets of the concern and are in possession of a
collateral security equal to the sum deposited,
which, by the way, bears interest. The scheme
is entirely novel, and we think the originators
deserve the support of the public in their efforts
to improve the position of the depositor. We
commend the above-mentioned pamphlet to the
attention of our readers who contemplate invest-
ing their money in this manner. It is forwarded
post free to any address by the bank, whose office
is at 301 Pitt-street, Sydney.*

NOBODY WANTS THAT GOLD RING.
For nearly 100 years a certain family of working
people living in Paris have ended their lives by
suicide. From father to sou, from mother to
daughter, has descended a plain gold ring, and on
the finger of every one of these suicides, as they
lay in death, this ring has been found. Only last
year the body of a young man who had killed
himself was brought to the Morgue, and on his
finger was the fatal golden circlet. H e was the
last of his race. The ring was buried with the
corpse, from which no one acquainted with its
history will have the courage to remove it.
The mental taint in this family came from some
remote ancestor, and was intensified by their
recognition of it until it became a controlling
force : and the ring was accepted as imposing upon
its possessor the obligation to commit suicide,
after the example of the person who last wore it.
This form of mania usually originates in a disorder
of the nervous system, which in its turn arises
from anaemia, or poverty of the blood, one of the
results of imperfect nutrition.
A recent letter from a gentleman living in Nor-
folk contains the following assertion : “/ longed
for death ; I was afraid of the night; I was afraid
to he alone, yet I hated society. I was afraid that
in some one of these hours oj deep gloom and depres-
sion I should lift my hand against my own life, for
I knew that many had done so from the same cause.”
The dark hours became a time of terror to him, so
he says. He tossed and tumbled on his bed,
wondering if morning would ever dawn again. In
this case it was not an accusing conscience, as he
had committed no offence ; the cause was purely
a physical one —yet, all too common in England—
indigestion and dyspepsia, with the long chain of
i consequences dragging after it, nervous collapse
among them.
He relates that his skin and eyes had been more
or less discoloured for years, often of a ghastly
and repulsive yellow. This was due to the presence
of bile in the blood and tissues, where it had no
business to be. But as the weak and torpid liver
could not remove it, no other result was possible
than the one our friend experienced. His head
frequently ached as though fiends had turned it
into a workshop, and pains chased one another
through his body as though he had at least half
the maladies catalogued in the popular books of
disease.
Yet one thing, and one only, was responsible
for all the mischief, namely, the poison introduced
into the blood from the decaying food in the
stomach and intestines. The cold feet, the loss
of appetite and ambition, the mental despondency,
the sense of weariness and fatigue, the bad taste
in the mouth, dry cough, giddiness, palpitation,
chills, weakness, &e., are a brood of foul birds
hatched in one nest, and the mother is always
indigestion and dyspepsia.
Time passed somehow, as it always does,
whether we laugh or cry, and this man grew
heartily tired of a life thus burdened and spoiled.
He longed to see the end of it, and no wonder.
But the last page of his letter is pitched in a
higher key. He says, “ When I think of what
I was, and what I am now, I can hardly realise
the change. For the past six months I have been
using a preparation known as Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup, and it has actually revolutionised
my whole system. One of my tenants recom-
mended it to me, and I tried it just to please him.
Now I praise it for myself, and thank the men
who made and advertise it. My troubles are over,
and I feel (at 57) as light, elastic, and gay as a
boy on his summer vacation. I tell my doctors
they are beaten at their own trade by an old
German nurse, and so far as I am concerned they
can’t deny it. I have no more horrible thoughts
of self-destruction, for I find too much enjoyment
in living. My thanks are too deep for words.”
The author of tins letter consents to the pub-
lication of so much of it as is here printed, but
declines to allow the use of his name, at least for
the present, for reasons we are bound to respect.
But the evident sincerity of his story will carry
conviction to every candid mind.

The New Dummyism.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
New South Wales dumipying is not done quite
so openly as it used to be ; but in the perpetual
struggle with the Land Act the squatter still
manages to keep well in the leach In the good
old times the game of grab was a comparatively
simple matter. Incurables from the hospitals
were sent forth to subdue the wilderness with
all the rations they needed in this world packed
inside a jerry-built dwelling, little roomier than
a coffin and scarcely more comfortable. The
generous squatter provided the necessary
capital, made out applications, and carefully
drilled his tools for the parts they had to play
before the land-court. Each man as he passed
the ordeal was carted out to the little sentry-
box provided for him, wherein he sat down to
enjoy the pleasure of being “settled on the
land,” as provided by the Act. The squatter’s
next care was to see the whole batch firmly
settled in the land. As a rule he waited
patiently for the reward of Christian charity.
These free selectors were guaranteed not to out-
last their rations, and all of them bequeathed
their little blocks to their benefactor in con-
sideration of unlimited rum and tobacco. Men
doomed by the doctors have been known to
recover, but in these cases rum and sheep-wash
tobacco rendered permanent recovery to health
impossible. Now the good old days when the
squatters acquired the earth by planting
derelicts “ ’neath the wattle ” have vanished.
* # «
Nowadays, the N.S. W. Land Act is very
constantly revised, and, therefore, just when the
philanthropic squatters have discovered the
loophole always left for them, a new Act
warranted dummy - proof is passed, and
again they groan, like the crushed
tragedian, “More Study!” Meantime, the
present Act is a perfect “throw-in” for the
lords of jumbuckdora. It allows the selector
who has served his sentence of “five years’
hard ” on a goat-paddock — i.e. , 40 acres, to
increase it to 2560 acres, in the Central Divi-
sion, without further residence. Presumably,
this clause was intended to apply solely to
the selector, who, having served his sentence on
40 acres, is entitled to increase it to the same
amount as the man whose capital enabled him
to start with the full area allowed by the Act,
but, in any case, it was not long before the
squatters commenced to work it for all it is
worth against natural enemy, the hona-Jlde
selector.
# # *
To old “Jacky” Hardswear, who devoted
every Sunday for years to the study of the Act,
the honour of discovering this loophole is due.
He bought up all the old forty-acre blocks in his
N.S.W. stations, and, on the strength of the
residence having been fulfilled by somebody,
alive or dead, applied for additional C.P.’s or
C.L.’s to the full amount to he added to each of
them. The applications were at first objected
to, but “Jacky” stood his ground upon the
strict wording of the clause, and triumphed. In
addition to other advantages this clause reduces
the cost and risk of dummying to a minimum.
In putting a dummy on a “ six-fortv ” the
squatter risked a fair amount of cash.
He required a man in whom the elements
of kna^ery and idiocy were equallv blended>
but ifc ' ften happened that the former
shifted the bean upset all the Bquattep . s
calculations . Apropos, the following true bill:
A Gwydip squ^ te " r uamed - well, Brown,
bought out a cockie (one Smith). After the
sale Brown asked Smith to dummy for him.
The grass parrot agreed, and with £BO of the
Bquatter > s inoney selected a paddock not far
from his home stead. Smith drew his “note
and rasllilis » regu larly, and spent most of his
time f encing and improving the land until the
five , residence had expired. Just about
the time when he got his certificate the squatter
, waB foolish enough to bully Mrs. Smith for
allowing some f rie nds to put their horses in the
paddock duril]g her h usb and’s absence. Smith
thereupon swore revenge. Whether he would
have transferred the itnd to his employer in
case is to doubt , but that he did not
is known to everyone in the district. He met
Brown outside the Land-office on the day he
had promise d to make the transfer, and said :
.. IVe sold n Bele ction, and here’s your share
of it .» Brown’s share was just £BO-without
interest and Smith still relates with pride how
he t .. a note a week and ra , sllu , s for im-
poundin - his own land y>
# # #
Mr Brown now employs only 40-acre dum-
mieB> If they “take him down ” he doesn’t
lose much, and if they run straight and “sell
out ” to him—they mostly do, the temptation
being small—Brown works the “ Jacky ” Hard-
swear clause, and sets the balance of the full
area added without' further residence. A few
40-acre dummies judiciously arranged along a
water-frontage will lock-up miles of the country
behind them, and scare away any “damned
selectah ” who hasn’t a thousand or two in hand
to spend upon tanks and wells,
* # #
A curious fact about dummying is that every-
body cau de tect it, save those who are paid by
Government to do so. Perjury before a Land
Board passes without comment, even when it is
practically admitted. The Board only occasion-
any refuses an application that has been made
[ n due form, for land available for selection,
when all the usual questions put to the appli-
cant on oath have been answered as required by
j aw . The refusal of any application simply
me ans that the would-be dummy perjured Uni-
self so clumsily that even the Board could not
avoid seeing it. Yet we rarely hear of such
men being punished for perjury. Let any care-
ful observer attend a bush Land Court through
a three days’ sitting and he will hear more
calm, cold-blooded perjury than all the Metro-
politan police-courts could produce in a year,
A shock-headed specimen, with no forehead to
speak of, stands up for his preliminary examina-
tion. He is a bush carpenter. Recognises the
cross at the foot of the application produced as
his signature (he is not asked who filled the
paper up for him). All the answers to the
printed questions thereon are correct. No one
asked him to take the land up. He has had no
business transactions with the lessees on the
run he is selecting upon, excepting payments
made to him for contract-work. He intends to

COCKATOO.
Matrimonial item :
Marriage, if not carried to excess, is a wise provision
and sacred obligation. Marry jour opposite as far as
possible, especially as regards sex. You will never regret
it. If possible, marry above your station. Both of j'ou
should do this. It is sure to advance your race. Do not
marry a foreigner unless highly recommended by those in
whom you have perfect confidence, or unites you want to
very much indeed. Do not encourage long engagements.
It is better to get Weary of each'other at j our leisure
after marriage than to do it beforehand. Courtship, how-
ever, is a most .delightful industry, and should not be
rashly broken in upon bj- marriage. Some people seem to
lie admirahlj-fitted for suitors, but faii in other occupa-
tions. Tins is very fortunate indeed. No suitor can be
sure of a permanent situation. The supply greatly exceeds
the demand.
Dear Bulletin, —You fall into the common
mistake of supposing that Home Rule without Parnell
must degenerate into Home Rule. As a Protestant, the
present member for Cork would ba the wrong leader in
the struggle which must inevitably arise between priests
and tire K.C. laity as soon ns Home Rule is granted. The
Irish, not half so'priest-ridden as the English, will deal
with .the Church as France did, but not under a Protes-
tant leader. Look at history. Before the Reformation
the Irish were the most turbulent of Catholics; in fact,
the English invasion was brought about at the i .stance of
the Rope. They had always been at variance with the
Vatican. But they refused to accept the Reformation at
England’s dictation. The more they were urged to break
away by outsiders the closer they clung to an allegiance
to which they had previously been lukewarm. Much the
same thing would happen now if Parnell were mad enough
to start an anti Papal crusade-and the man seems mad
enough for anything. Which reminds me that Baron
Congleton, the most illustrious of the Parnell family,
committed suicide, and that other members died lunatic's.'
Don’t make a mistake in thinking that men like Dillon",
Sexton, O’Brien, Heidv, the M'Carthys, the O’Connors
(Arthur and T. P.), &c., will ever be priest-ridden,—
Yours, Onk Who Knows Them

The Disappearance of Molloy.
AN INCONCLUSIVE STORY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Down on the Starlight Flat, at Christmas-
tide five-and-thirty years ago, the leading store
was that of a firm named Grant and Molloy, who
did a thriving trade and were the largest gold-
buyers on the field.
Grant was a widower with only one child, an
attractive girl of 18 or 19 years, with a wealth of
brown hair and loving blue eyes—and, better still,
a generous and kindly disposition. Nelly Grant
was her father’s housekeeper, comforter, and in-
separable companion, and, in truth, the very
apple of his eye. An apple which, it goes without
saying, was sorely coveted by all the young
fellows in the neighbourhood; and endless were
the cunning excuses invented for a visit to the
store, just for the chance of a bright smile or
pleasant greeting from pretty Nellie. But, al-
though the young girl smiled kindly upon her
host of earnest wooers, she seemed insensible to
the blandishments of all, save one —young Tom
Daley.
Tom was a fine young fellow of six or seven-
and-twenty. He had been a clerk in a Melbourne
house for some years previous to the opening cf
the “ Starlight,” but, like thousands of others,
unable to resist the temptations of the glovying
accounts constantly arriving from the new field,
he had thrown up his situation and hastened off
to the “ rush,” hopeful of falling across one of the
big nuggets or rich patches of which he had heard
so much. And, although it was an open secret at
the “Nob” that he had garnered a better treasure
than either in the shape of Nellie’s love, his ill-
success in gold-digging was a sad damper to his
happiness ; for Tom was poor—and proud—and
far too fond of the girl to speak out just yet. But
Tom worked away steadily, in the hope of “strik-
ing it heavy ” some day soon.
# # #
Although Starlight Flat had been given a
marvellous riddling, and was now, in the main,
merely a “hatter’s” diggings, there were yet
many good patches only waiting some lucky stroke
of the pick; outside “ streaks” still undiscovered;
Carelessly-worked ground and so forth, for the
held had been as extensive as rich, and Tom had
good grounds for his hopes.
Being Christinas time, all hands had “washed
up” and disposed of their gold, in preparation for
the coming festivities. A large quantity had been
Purchased by “Grant and Molloy,” including
some very fine nuggets, in all amounting to
2500 z. Now, the nearest bank being in a town
distant some miles or so from the “Nob,” it was
Grant’s custom to drive thither himself periodi-
cally, sell his gold, and return with the money on
the following day. On this particular occasion,
however, it was arranged that Molloy, the junior
Partner, should go in his stead. Accordingly on
Boxing Day morning, 185 G, the gold was emptied
>nto a large scoop, sifted and thoroughly cleansed.
and finally weighed and returned to the chamois
and sealed. All this took place at the open
counter in the presence of Tom Daley and Nellie,
who were admiring the strange shapes of the
nuggets, and conversing with Molloy as he sifted
the fine gold while Grant was passing to and fro
attending to the customers, who proved to be
pretty numerous on that particular morning.
Whilst in the vicinity of the trio his attention was
directed by Nellie to a very remarkable-looking
“speck” which Molloy had just weighed and
declared to be 2dwts. It was a thin, flat nugget,
hollow in the centre, and in shape exactly
resembled a heart.
# * *
Grant took it into his hand and examined
it closely. He was just remarking that he had
not observed it before, when he was called off by
a customer, and the incident passed away from
his memory. An hour later the gold having been
put into a valise and properly secured in the boot
of the buggy, Molloy, armed with a brace of
revolvers, took his departure for the bank.
Two days after, the buggy was picked up on
the ranges with bits of broken harness attached to
it, and brought to the store. Grant, in great con-
sternation, at once collected a party of volunteers,
whom he despatched to scour the country in a all
directions in search of Molloy, whilst he him-
self rode away to town in order to ascertain what
time his partner had left on his return journey.
Upon enquiring at the bank he found, to his
surprise, that Molloy had never presented himself
there, neither had anyone in the township seen or
heard anything of him. Interviewing the
authorities, he communicated all the circum-
stances of the case, and being assured by the
Inspector that no time would be lost in making a
thorough investigation, there was nothing else to
be done but return home and see if anything fresh
had happened in that quarter. On arrival he
learned that nothing had been seen in any way to
account for the disappearance of Molloy and his
valise. Poor Grant was at his wits’ end. He had
a sincere regard for his partner, who had been in
his employ for a number of years previous to his
receiving a small interest in the business, and
never questioned his probity, therefore he
had little doubt that he had been murdered
for the sake of the gold by some of the
numerous out-throats who infested the bush.
The loss of so large a sum of money was,
however, a serious matter, and would certainly
cripple his business, for, though well above the
world, he was by no means wealthy. However,
there was nothing to be done but to await with
patience the result of the investigations of the
police, but, though neither time nor trouble was
spared in the endeavour to discover the missing
man, no trace could be found. Molloy had dis-
appeared as effectually as if he had been spirited
away; and as time sped on the diggers at the
“Nob’’ceased to speculate on the matter, until
at length, like many other mysteries, it became
“forgotten history.”
11.
The Heart-shaped Speck.
Twelve months had passed away and
Christmas was again at hand. Tom Daley’s suit
had prospered amazingly, for he and Nelly were
to be married early in the coming year. Tom
had —in digging parlance —“struck the jugular,”
3oz. to the tub, no less. The ground was virgin,
and but six feet deep, and, having no mate, lie
had the claim all to himself. Of course, the whole
community had swarmed around him ; hundreds
of shafts had been sunk, but not a single one of
them had bottomed on gold ; no, hardly even so
much as the “ colour.” Such a completely isolated
patch was hitherto unexampled in Starlight 3*lat.
So, after riddling and cutting the ground in alt
directions without finding gold, the diggers bad
reluctantly “given it best,’’and for the last few
weeks Tom had been sole occupant of the ground.
The patch, although very rich, had, however, ex-
tended over but a small area of ground, and he
had now nearly worked it out. He had just
handed to Grant the result of his last week’s work,
and passed through the store_ into the dwelling-
house behind to have a chat with Nelly, while her
father washed and cleaned the gold and weighed
it. Grant emptied the gold into a scoop and pro-
ceeded to shake it about and examine it in the’
usual way. It was a coarse, shotty sample, in-
cluding a number of biggish specks of various
sizes ; some of them would have to go into the’
mortar presently, having bits of ironstone attached.
All at once he laid down the scoop, and, going
to the passage communicating with the house,
listened. Sounds of low rippling laughter were
wafted to his ear. The young couple were having
a good time. He made a movement as if he would
pass through and join them ; then turned, closed
the door, and went back to the scoop. Picking up
one of the bigger bits of gold he held it up to the
light and scrutinised it minutely, then put it into'
the scale and weighed it—it was exactly two-
pennyweights. Beyond a doubt, it was the’
“ heart-shaped speck.”
The whole scene of 12 months ago came vividly
before him. He recollected clearly the weight of
the “ speck ” and his own curious examination of
it, every particular stood out as clear as in a vision,
but—win nee this fearful re-appearance of the’
heart-shaped speck ?
A rush of hideous ideas took his breath away
and made his brain reel. Whilst trying to 1
recover himself and collect his thoughts the door
of the passage opened and Tom entered with a
smile. “ Well,” he began, “how about the gold?'
But,” he continued, “I see you are busy, and
won’t interrupt you. I’ll be over early to-morrow
to take Nell to the picnic.” And with a cheery
look in his eyes Tom passed out, giving his future
father-in-law a friendly nod as he went.
But Grant, who, on hearing Tom’s step, hastily
opened his ledger, averted his face, and uttered 1 ,
never a word. He moved slowly to the doorway,
and leaning wearily against the sill looked after
the slight young figure stopping briskly along in
the sunshine. He went back carefully over his
two years’ acquaintance with Tom ; remembered
his unvarying generosity and self-denial; and,
above all, his manly, straightforward conduct in
his relations with Nellie; and as hedwelton these
two things, his mind grew calmer, and the fright-
ful suspicions so suddenly engendered by his
recognition of the little nugget seemed out of all
reason j little short of madness indeed. Still,
supposing Tom to be free from all complicity ini
the robbery—if not murder—of the missing
Molloy, what theory would possibly account for
the possession of the piece of gold ? Sorely exer-
cised by these conflicting conjectures, he turned!
to his books, and, adding up the amount of gold
which he had purchased from Tom during the last
two months found, to his further consternation,
that it weighed within a trifle of 2500z5., the exact
quantity carried aioay by Molloy.
Pondering upon this most ominous coincidence,
the singularly isolated character of Tom’s
“ patch ” recurred to him in a new and convincing
aspect. Dwelling upon these dircfully significant
incidents, a multitude of trifling circumstances
flashed through his mind—trifling when considers
by themselves ; but which, read in the lurid ligh
of later developments, united in forging such a
chain of evidence against Tom as would surely
admit of only one interpretation. The whole
tory seemed clear enough now. Tom’s cupidity,
aroused by the sight of the large quantity of gold,
the comparative ease by which he might become
possessed of it, thus removing the chief obstacle
to the gratification of his love; his interception
and murder of the unfortunate Molloy near the
deserted old workings, and his ready disposal of
the body in one of the deep, wet holes which, in
the nature of things, would prove a sure and un-
discoverable grave; and finally his pretended
discovery in “ Duffer’s Gully,” as it had been
dubbed by the disappointed diggers. Then, as to
his inexplicable retention and disposal of the
“ small nugget,” of whose easy identification he
must have been aware—it could only be another
of those unaccountable oversights so common in
the most ingeniously-concerted crimes ; confirming
the truth of the old saying that “ murder will
out.” Great Heavens! what a providential escape
for his child !
Then the thought of the probable effect upon
poor Nellie, of the dreadful revelation, brought a
tear to his eye. But whatever unfortunate conse-
quences might ensue, his course was clear and
inevitable. He must prevent all future communi-
cation between his daughter and Tom, and take
steps to have the latter arrested at once. Reason-
ing thus, but loth to make the first move, Grant
paced backward and forward in front of the store
all the afternoon, nodding mechanically once in a
way to some passing acquaintance, and deli-
berating anxiously as to the best way of breaking
the news to his daughter.
* « •
Tom Daley, unconscious of the tempest
■of emotions rending the breast of his prospective
father-in-law, was unwittingly about to precipi-
tate matters in an entirely unsuspected way. He
had eaten his supper and was busily engaged in
ransacking the pockets of sundry cast-off trousers
and waistcoats in search of some article, the
absence of which seemed to puzzle him greatly,
when all of a sudden he dropped the old clothes
and, putting on his hat, started for the store.
Although still early, on arrival, somewhat to his
surprise he found it closed ; but this, as may be
;supposed, was a matter of small moment to Tom,
who betook himself to the door of the private
•dwelling, and knocked.
* * *
Grant had, in the interim, written a note
to the Inspector of Police, requesting his presence
at as early a date as might be convenient, in order
to receive some important information in reference
to the mysterious disappearance of his late partner,
Molloy. This, he felt, was as far as he could
bring himself to go in the matter at present, for in
spite of the manifest guilt of Tom Daley, so great
was the conflict in his mind that the initial step
he had taken to bring him to justice had been
attended by some sharp pangs of remorse. He
felt quite unequal to the task of broaching the
subject to his daughter, and being completely
tired out, had closed the store and retired to his
room in search of sleep.
• * •
On being admitted, Tom found Nellie and
a companion deeply immersed in the congenial
occupation of inspecting and discussing the rela-
tive merits of a number of articles of feminine
finery strewn upon the table, and explained as the
cause of his unexpected visit that he had mislaid

Attorney Sam. Gillott objected in the Mel-
bourne District Court the other day to the term,
“Suffered to be drunk upon the premises,” on the
ground that one generally drank first and suffered
afterwards.

his long-treasured speck, that he had searched for
it everywhere without success, and that as a last
resource he had come to see if by any chance it
had got amongst the gold he had left with her
father in the morning. Nellie rose sympatheti-
cally and went to enquire. She was gone, Tom
thought, for an unusfial length of time for so sim-
ple an errand, and on her return her face bore a
strangely thoughtful and puzzled expression. She
was followed immediately by her father, who,
somewhat to that young gentleman’s surprise,
shook hands with an unexampled exuberance and
warmth. _ After a long and pleasant evening’s
conversation, Tom returned to his own little homo
highly elated in spirits, and with the fateful speck
snugly secured in his pocket.
* # #
It is only needful to say that the substance
of Nellie’s communication to her father was to the
effect that the “ heart-shaped speck ” had been in
Tom’s possession for many months prior to the
despatch of Molloy with the gold ; that he had,
at Nellie’s request, shown it to the missing man,
who had put it into the scales out of curiosity,
and that if Grant had not been so suddenly called
away he would have learned all about it.
* * #
Tom and Nellie were married in due
course, and although neither of them ever under-
stood the mental suffering it had cost Grant, the
heart-shaped speck is still in their possession, and
will doubtless be handed down to generations of
Daleys indefinitely. Many years ago a rumour
reached Grant, to the effect that his partner had
been met with in New Zealand by some old
acquaintance fropi the “ Nob,” but, otherwise,
nothing authentic has ever transpired to account
in any way for the disappearance of Molloy.
J. LYALL.
Maxim, the Yankee gun-inventor, has
built, or is building, the long-desired flying-
machine, which is to overturn the world. It is to
be a species of very light locomotive, and when it
starts on its wild career will run along the surface
of the earth like any other vehicle. At 25 miles
an hour, however, it will begin to jump and mani-
fest a desire to rise; at 30 miles, it will skim
along, only grazing the earth occasionally ; and at
35 miles it will rear up and strrt into the air. The
inventor says : “If I can rise from the coast of
France, sail through the air across the Channel,
and drop half-a-ton of nitro glycerine upon an
English city, I can revolutionise the world. ” This
seems an eminently probable statement, too.
Half-a-ton of nitro-glycerine, dropped in the right
place, would wipe out any empire nowadays.
One of the curiosities of Sydney is an
eminent legal luminary, who may be seen any
day trotting contentedly down the street, with
papers falling promiscuously out of all his pockets.
He is full of papers and manuscript and all sorts
of written matter, and he slops over with them,
and sheds them wherever he goes. Anybody can
make him drop about eighteen writs and duns
and lawyers’ letters by simply rushing a horse at
him when he is negotiating a busy crossing, and
making him run, for when he runs it shakes the
literature out of him. The people who pick up
the documents generally grin a broad, capacious
grin, and carry them round to the nearest news-
paper-office. Twice this week the legal gentleman
bolted across Pitt-street, and each time the bill he
dropped was brought to The Bulletin. The first
time it was a liquor bill for £lO or so, with a
peremptory memo, at the foot demanding the
cash. The next time it was the same liquor bill,
with a more peremptory memo, at the foot, stating
that the firm’s lawyer was about to “ take pro-
ceedings.” We are now waiting for the old man
to run across the street a third time, and drop the
writ, and then the series will be complete.

An Evening in Dandaloo.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was while we held our races
Hurdles, sprints and steeplechases—
Up in Dandaloo,
That a crowd of Sydney stealers,
Jockeys, pugilists and spielers
Brought some horses, real heelers,
Came and put us through.
Beat our nags and won our money.
Made the game by no means funny,
Made us rather blue ;
When the racing was concluded,
Of our hard-earned coin denuded
Dandaloonies sat and brooded
There in Dandaloo.
• .»•••«
Night came down on Johnson’s shanty
Where the grog was no means scanty,
And a tumult grew
Till some wild, excited person
Galloped down the township cursing,
“ Sydney push have mobbed Macpherson,
Roll up, Dandaloo ! ”
Great St. Denis ! what commotion !
Like the rush of stormy ocean
Riery horsemen flew.
Dust and smoke and din and rattle,
Down the street they spurred their cattle
To the war-cry of the battle,
“Wade in, Dandaloo ! ”
So the boys might have their fight out,
Johnson blew the bar-room light out.
Then, in haste, withdrew.
And in darkness and in doubting
Raged the conflict and the shouting,
“ Give the Sydney push a clouting,
“ Go it, Dandaloo !”
Jack Macpherson seized a bucket,
Every head he saw he struck it—
Struck in earnest, too;
And a man from Lower Wattle,
Whom a shearer tried to throttle,
Hit out freely with a bottle,
There in Dandaloo.
Skin and hair were flying thickly,
When a light was fetched, and quickly
Brought a fact to view—
On the scene of the diversion
Every single, solid person
Came along to help Macpherson—
AH were Dandaloo !
When the list of slain was tabled,
Sonic were drunk and some disabled,
Still we found it true.
In the darkness and the smother
We’d been belting one another,
Jack Macpherson bashed his brother,
There in Dandaloo.
So we drank, and all departed—
How the “ mobbing” yarn was started
No one ever knew —
And the stockmen tell the story
Of that conflict fierce and gory,
How We fought for love and glory
Up in Dandaloo.
It’s a proverb now, or near it—
At the races you can hear it,
At the dog-fights, too ;
Every shrieking, dancing drover
As the canines topple over,
Yells applause to Grip or Rover,
“ Give him ‘ Dandaloo ’! ’
And the teamster slowly toiling
Through the deep black country soiling
Wheels and axles, too,
Lays the whip on Spot and Banker,
Rouses Tarboy with a flanker—
‘Redman ! Ginger 1 Heave there ! Yank her !”
“ Wade in, Dandaloo !”
B.
The New Par by.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the track where travellers so,
Silent and sad and slow,
By the well where the drovers rest,
In the sun of the hot North-West;
On the plains where the stockmen meet,
By the camp-fire’s dying light;
On the hills where the miners throng,
In the city’s busy street.
In the daytime, in the night,
A voice comes clear and strong—
And the traveller halts in his stride,
Though but half the day be done ;
And the horsemen pause in their ride
In the blaze of the noonday sun.
In the heart of the city’s life,
On the trackless plain,
Workers waging their strife
For a pitiful gain ;
Hest for a minute then,
At the sound of the voice.
“Listen, O men, that are men,
In your hearts rejoice.
“ To-day is the day of days,” it said,
“ For the people have broken their thrall,
And hopes are living that erst were dead,
And lies, in the countless ages bred,
Are doomed in their time to fall.
Vo have waited long, not taking heed,
Whilst traitors misruled in your place ;
But right prevails in the hour of need,
And a brutal law and a rich man’s greed
Ye have fought them face to face.
Fought them and beaten them. Who says nay?
Though the battle be not yet won,
Ve have struggled free from a tyrant sway—
Be true to yourselves as ye have this day,
And the rule of misrule is done.
“The future is yours, and the power
_ To make and unmake and reign ;
But success comes not in an hour,
And is victory bought without pain?
Struggle on, struggle on to the goal,
Ye shall lead who have long been led.”
And the workers listened with steadfast soul—
“ 'Ve will conquer or die,” they said.
GEORGE DANT.

Te Anau Lake.
The following verses are said to have been com-
posed on the lake at Te Anau, N.Z., by “an
eminent Australian lawyer.” They are (this
isn’t saying much, perhaps) the best verses yet by
any Australian lawyer, that we know of, from
Judgoßarron Field down :
The wistful waters wander at the base
Of Anau’s brawny breast:
The virgin snow enclasps with cold embrace
His towering crest.
The tall hills stand along the watery way
As guards before a king:
Rob’d with soft foliage where sunbeams play,
And shadows cling.
Here the forgotten gods descend in mist
To bathe a sunny hour,
And softly lave their white wings where they kiss’d
The Rata’s flower.
I know not whether tenor ruled thy birth
O ! lake of Titian face,
Or beauty thought to bring at once to earth
Her every grace ;
Or both combined, to show to mortal eyes
How deep the mystery
Which living things and dead things imdeil'. s
Eternally;
And how, beyond the music of the tide,
The vvitcher3’ °f the snow
The gold of sunset on the mountain si.ia
And woods below.
The full-voiced whistle of the rising lark,
The torrents’ sullen roar,
Is that which must remain a riddle dark
For evermore.
Oh ! lake ; illusion was the name so meet,
They gave thy bosom fair,
And near, thy note of sorrow doth repeat
Thy deep despair.
Joe Chamberlain summed up :
Joseph Chamberlain smiles ; he does not laugh. Ilis
smile is peculiarly unsatisfactory, as if an adder had taken
to grinning. Mr. Chamberlain, too, lias a wandering and
uneasy eye, and a habit of glancing furtively around him,
as if he expected each moment to be arrested. Chamber-
lain is chiefly known by his bitter advocacy of the coercion
of the Irish democracy. He began life as a debating
society Tory. Then he became a Republican ; and now he
has re-joined the Tory party. All his inclinations, man-
ners, and dealings savour of the shoddy middle-class
whose god is wealth, and who look upon Heaven as a
place where special stalls are reserved for themselves.
The sky will fall when anybody trusts Mr. Chamberlain,
but the sky is not likely to fall.
France and England have entered into a
solemn agreement to respect each other’s mail-
boats in time of war, that the widows and orphans
of either side may promptly receive the dying
messages of the respective heaps of slain. This is
a genteel but utterly mischievous compact, because
the original idea in going to war was to make the
enemy sick, and any practice which tends to
upset commerce, and make both enemies sick as
soon as possible, ought to be maintained, not
abolished. Governments seem to want to trim
and polish campaigns, and wipe up the gore with
a clean wet flannel, so as to give them quite a
decent appearance. Out upon such sugar-coated
barbarism ! Give us one good war in all its
native hideousness - prisoners burnt alive, the
wounded left to rot, women and children tortured
to death, every possible house, crop, bridge, dumb
animal, and parson destroyed—and civilisation
would cry off battles once and forever. The
modern majah - general with his talk about
chivalry and pretence of converting wars into
picnics, is a contemptible beast as compared with
a naked Zulu, who shoves his spear into every
available member of the nation that is trying to
wipe him out.
There was a large, fat, sleepy individual in
the Melbourne express the other night, and he
had a ticket for Wodonga, which was the next
stopping place. He looked like a man who
intended to wake up and get his traps together at
the last moment, but meanwhile he lay in a
reposeful attitude on the seat. His boots were off,
to ease his corns most likely, and he had seven
packages, and a gun, and an overcoat. Presently
he woke with a frantic start to find the train just
stopping. Then he evidently concluded that he
had been sleeping for the last twenty miles, and he
made a grab at the luggage, and tried to get a
boot on one foot and a parcel on the other. Next
he dropped the parcel and grabbed for the other
boot, and the parcel burst through the fall, and
scattered a quantity of underclothing ail over the
place. He got the boots on the wrong feet, and
shoved the underclothing in his pockets, and
grabbed for the gun and some more parcels.
Just then the train started again, and with a
whoop of rage and excitement he launched him-
self at the door, amid a perfect cataract of
packages all bursting open in mid-air. He
landed on the platform in a sitting position, and
surveyed the wreckage. The gun was damaged,
and the overcoat had a tear down the back ; bis
hat was left behind in the train; a quantity of tea
and other provisions were spilt around loosely on
the platform; it was raining like thunder, and
there was no town in sight. The express had
stopped for a moment to breathe, and he had
thrown himself out into an empty wilderness
thirteen miles from anywhere in particular, and
there he was left—a distracted fat man in a great
desolation of grass, with never a bed or a pub or a
square meal in sight. I

The Tongan Parliament has abolished all
legal redress for debt. In that favoured land
there are now no more bills or p.n’s or 1.0 IPs, for
they are waste paper, every one. If anybody lets
anybody else have goods on tick he must trust to
Providence for the cash. There are no more time-
payment pianos or sewing-machines, or cheap
instalment furniture, or drinks that are chalked
up ; and nobody can go bankrupt, for to go bank-
rupt a man must owe something, and under the
new law nobody can owe anything in all Tonga.
It is a good and wise device, and the white man
should go and do likewise. Then there would be
no more calls recoverable in wild-cat mines, no
more land-speculation booms, no more part-books
or deferred-payment allotments, no more bills of
sale, no more flaring chromos. sold to the deluded
suburban female, to be paid for at Is. per weak,
no more anything, in fact; and everybody would
have a rest —a long, long, peaceful rest.

J. W. Lindt, of Melbourne, is going for a
trip to the Caroline Islands. When you are at
the Carolines it is prudent, ere you go ashore for a
walk, to entice on board your ship three or four
native chiefs, and let them know that unless you
are permitted to return alive they will be hanged
at the yard arm by the crew. Otherwise, you'are
very liable to be eaten. Should the nigs, eat
Lindt, they will, on the heathen theory that you
incorporate the spiritual qualities of the enemy
on whom you have dined, have a very high opinion
of themselves indeed.

The man Sheridan, now in Darlinghurst
prison, where he has been for 18 years—since boy.
hood —is to be released at the end, of two years,
which means that his life-sentence will be 20 years.
Sheridan has never yet set eyes upon the sea.

“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletsn,”
Premier A mtralian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dig
tinctive Australian National Policy. 1
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
ißate Revenue 'derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
-Ihe Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
■ The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
Ihe Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
ar-d has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established .Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claiu’i
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the moSt
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official— that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than anv
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, blit I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter jT
was expectoratingevery day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
©nd of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytub, J.P.
